Tasawwur and Tasdiq

8 min
Razi's Argument for the Necessity of Conceptualization in Causal Knowledge
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that knowledge of the cause alone does not give knowledge of the effect without conceptualization of the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowledge of a cause alone is insufficient for knowledge of its effect, using the concept of relational attributes ('amr idafi).

Core Idea
Causality is relational; knowing one relatum does not give knowledge of the relation without conceptualizing the other.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi challenges a common assumption: that knowing a cause's essence automatically yields knowledge of its effect. In this passage from his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat, he argues that causality is a relational attribute ('amr idafi) requiring both relata to be mentally present. This lesson unpacks Razi's argument and its implications for how we acquire knowledge of causal relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
Razi is arguing against the position that knowledge of the essence of the cause (māhiyyat al-‘illa) is sufficient to know the effect (ma‘lūl). Locate where he states this in the Arabic text.
The phrase 'knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect' is his target.
2
Extract Razi's key premise
Razi states that causality (al-‘illiyya) is a relational matter (amr idāfī). He argues that relational matters cannot be established by a single thing; both relata must be present.
Note that he uses 'al-‘illiyya' (causality) and 'al-ma‘lūliyya' (effectuality) as the relational descriptions.
3
Understand the nature of relational attributes
Relational attributes (al-umūr al-iḍāfiyya) are those that depend on two relata. For example, fatherhood requires both father and child. Similarly, causality requires both cause and effect.
Think of other examples: brotherhood requires two brothers, location requires a thing and a place.
4
Follow the logical consequence
If the cause's essence alone cannot produce the attribute of causality, then knowledge of that essence cannot generate knowledge of causality, and thus cannot lead to knowledge of the effect.
Razi's reasoning: causality not obtained from cause alone → knowledge of cause alone insufficient for knowledge of causality → insufficient for knowledge of effect.
5
Identify the condition for relational knowledge
Razi concludes that when both the cause and effect are conceptualized (tasawwur), the assent (tasdiq) to their relational connection necessarily follows. Thus, conceptualization of both is necessary.
This aligns with the epistemological distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq: you need conceptualization of the relata before you can assent to the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول هذه الشكوك وإن حللناها في كتابنا الكبير لكنا نقول : العلم بماهية العلة لا يقتضي العلم بالمعلول إلا بشرط آخر وهو حصول تصور المعلول. بيانه وهو أنَّ ماهية العلَّة وحدها لا تكفي في حصول العليَّة، لأنَّ العليَّة أمر إضافي والأمور الإضافية لا تكفي في حصولها الشَّيء الواحد، بل لا بدَّ من حصول كلا المضافين. وإذا لم تكن ذات العلة مستقلة باقتضاء حصول صفة العلية لا جرم لم يكن العلم بذات العلة كافيًا في حصول العلم بالعليَّة. وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن العلم بذات العلة موجبا لحصول العلم بالمعلول. فأمَّا ذات العلة وذات المعلول فإنهما لذاتيهما يقتضيان الوصف الإضافي وهو العلية والمعلولية، لا جرم عند حصول تصوريهما يجب حصول التصديق بانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر
We say: these doubts, even if we have resolved them in our comprehensive book, we say: knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect except with another condition, namely obtaining a conceptualization of the effect. Its explanation: the essence of the cause alone is not sufficient for the occurrence of causality, because causality is a relational matter, and relational matters are not sufficient in their occurrence by a single thing, rather it is necessary that both relata occur. And if the essence of the cause is not independent in requiring the occurrence of the attribute of causality, then necessarily knowledge of the essence of the cause is not sufficient for obtaining knowledge of causality. And if this is the case, then knowledge of the essence of the cause does not lead to obtaining knowledge of the effect. As for the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect, they by themselves necessitate the relational description, which is causality and effectuality, so necessarily when their conceptualizations are obtained, the assent to the relation of one to the other must occur.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an excerpt from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat (Sharh al-Isharat). In it, Razi argues against the claim that knowledge of the essence of a cause alone is sufficient to know the effect. He insists that an additional condition is required: the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the effect. The reason is that causality is a relational attribute ('amr idafi). Relational attributes cannot be established by a single entity; both relata must be present. To have causality, both cause and effect must exist. Thus, knowing the cause's essence does not automatically yield knowledge of causality. Only when both the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are conceptualized does the assent (tasdiq) to their relational connection—causality and effectuality—necessarily follow. This argument underscores the epistemological principle that relational knowledge requires the mental presence of both terms of the relation.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
تصور
تصديق
أمر إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is knowledge of the cause alone insufficient to know the effect?
A) The effect is unknowable.
B) Causality is a relational matter requiring both relata.
C) The cause must be known through demonstration.
D) The effect must be perceived.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a relational attribute requires conceptualization of both relata; thus, to know causality, one must conceptualize both the cause and the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Kalam proof: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Using Razi's argument, identify which concepts (tasawwurat) must be present for the assent to the causal relation, and explain why knowing only the first premise does not suffice.
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, a jurist uses analogy (qiyas) to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far‘) based on an effective cause (‘illa). Explain how the‘illa is a relational attribute between the asl and the far‘, and why conceptualization of both cases is necessary before the analogy can be valid.

Razi's Correction of Ibn Sina's Definition of Possibility
Critique of Ibn Sina's definition of possibility and its correction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in Ibn Sina's definition of possibility and state Razi's correction.

Core Idea
Possibility must account for both sides: existence and non-existence.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi critiques Ibn Sina's definition of possibility from the Isharat. Ibn Sina defined possibility as 'what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence.' Razi points out that this definition is too narrow, as the possibility of non-existence also falls under the broader notion. He then provides a more comprehensive definition. Understanding this critique sharpens your grasp of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Sina's definition
Find the exact wording of Ibn Sina's definition as presented by Razi. It is: 'الإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم' (Possibility either means what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence).
2
Identify Razi's objection
Razi argues that this definition is too narrow because it only covers possibility of existence. The phrase 'it can not be' applies also to the impossible and the specific possible, showing that possibility must include both sides.
Note that 'it can not be' in this sense is predicated of the impossible as well, so the definition is too restricted.
3
Extract Razi's proposed definition
Razi states: 'فالواجب أن يقال: الإمكان ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في أحد جانبي الوجود والعدم' (So it must be said: possibility is what entails the negation of necessity in one of the two sides of existence and non-existence).
4
Compare the two definitions
Ibn Sina's definition isolates only the existence side, while Razi's includes both sides. Razi's is more comprehensive and avoids the error of explaining the general by the specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول صراحة: [ واعلم أنَّ في لفظ الشَّيخ هي هنا نوع إشكال؛ لأنَّه قال: الإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، فقد خصَّص هذا الإمكان بما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وليس كذلك؛ فإن هذا الإمكان حاصل أيضًا في سلب ضرورة الوجود، ألا ترى أنَّ إمكان العدم بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص؛ لأن قولك يمكن أن لا يكون بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص، فالإمكان إذن أعم من الإمكان في الوجود أو الإمكان في العدم، والذي يلزم سلب ضرورة العدم هو إمكان الوجود لا الإمكان المطلق، فكأنَّه فسَّر العام بالخاص. فالواجب أن يقال: الإمكان ما يلازم سلب الضَّرورة في أحد جانبي الوجود والعدم، فإن كان في جانب الوجود فهو الذي يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وإنْ كان في جانب العدم فهو الذى يلازم سلب ضرورة الوجود. قال: وإما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود إلى آخره؛ أقول: قد عرفت أن الممكن العام هو الذي يصدق عليه عدم الضَّرورة في أحد طرفي الوجود أو العدم، والممكن الخاص هو الذى يصدق عدم الضّرورة على طرفيه معًا، فحينئذ يكون الممكن العام صادقًا على طرفي الممكن الخاص، فلأجل ذلك نقلوا اسم الممكن إليه من المعنى الأوّل والواجب خارج عن هذا الممكن والقسمة بحسبه ثلاثة: إما واجبة، أو ممكنة، أو ممتنعة. ولفظ الكتاب هي هنا أيضًا مشعر بأنَّ الإمكان الخاص ليس هو نفس سلب الضّرورتين، بل هو أمر يلازم ذلك السلب، ونحن قد بيّنا فى كتابنا الكبير أنَّه يمتنع أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيّا. وهذا البحث وإن كان غير لائق بالمنطق لكنا نحتاج إليه لاستخراج فوائد ألفاظ الكتاب.
And he says explicitly: [Know that in the Master's wording here there is a type of problem; because he said: 'Possibility either means what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence,' so he restricted this possibility to what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence, but it is not so; for this possibility also occurs in the negation of the necessity of existence. Do you not see that the possibility of non-existence in this sense is predicated of the impossible and the specific possible? Because your statement 'it can not be' in this sense is predicated of the impossible and the specific possible. So possibility then is more general than possibility in existence or possibility in non-existence. What entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence is the possibility of existence, not absolute possibility. So it is as if he explained the general by the specific. So it must be said: Possibility is what entails the negation of necessity in one of the two sides of existence and non-existence. If it is on the side of existence, then it is what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence; if it is on the side of non-existence, then it is what entails the negation of the necessity of existence. He said: 'Or it means what entails the negation of necessity in non-existence and existence' etc. I say: You have known that the general possible is that which has the negation of necessity in one of the two sides of existence or non-existence, and the specific possible is that which has the negation of necessity on both sides together. Then the general possible is true of both sides of the specific possible. For that reason they transferred the name 'possible' to it from the first meaning. The necessary is outside this possible, and the division accordingly is three: either necessary, or possible, or impossible. The wording of the book here also indicates that the specific possibility is not itself the negation of the two necessities, but rather a thing that entails that negation. And we have explained in our large book that it is impossible for it to be a positive thing. This investigation, although not suitable for logic, we need it to extract the benefits of the wording of the book.]
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of Ibn Sina's definition of possibility (imkan) as found in the Isharat. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) points out an ambiguity: Ibn Sina defined possibility as 'what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence.' Razi argues that this definition is too narrow, because the possibility of non-existence (i.e., contingency of not-existing) also falls under possibility. In fact, the phrase 'it can not be' applies not only to the specific possible (which can both be and not be) but also to the impossible (which necessarily does not exist) in the sense that it is possible for it not to be. So the definition must be more general. Razi proposes that possibility is the negation of necessity in one of the two sides: if the negation is on the side of existence, it corresponds to possibility of existence (negation of necessity of non-existence); if on the side of non-existence, it corresponds to possibility of non-existence (negation of necessity of existence). Then he discusses the distinction between general possible (mumkin 'amm) and specific possible (mumkin khass). General possible is when one side has no necessity; specific possible is when both sides have no necessity. The general possible includes both the side of existence and the side of non-existence of the specific possible. Historically, the term 'possible' was transferred from the specific meaning to the general. The necessary (wajib) is outside this division, so everything is either necessary, possible (in the general sense), or impossible. Finally, Razi notes that the specific possibility is not simply the negation of two necessities (i.e., a privative concept), but something that entails that negation; however, he has shown in another book that it cannot be a positive entity. This discussion, though meta-logical, helps understand the language of the text.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الممكن العام
الممكن الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the correct definition of possibility?
A) Negation of necessity of non-existence.
B) Negation of necessity in one of the two sides of existence and non-existence.
C) Negation of both necessities.
D) Affirmation of contingency.

Key Takeaway
Razi corrects Ibn Sina by showing that possibility must be defined as negation of necessity on either side, not just on the side of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which part of Ibn Sina's definition Razi argues is too narrow and explain why it fails to cover cases like the possibility of non-existence of the impossible.

General Possible vs. Specific Possible
Clarification of general and specific possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general possible (mumkin 'amm) and specific possible (mumkin khass) and explain their logical relationship.

Core Idea
General possible is broader: it includes both sides of specific possible.

After correcting the definition, Razi clarifies the distinction between two types of possibility: general and specific. The general possible applies when one side lacks necessity, while the specific possible requires both sides to lack necessity. This distinction is crucial for understanding modal classifications in Islamic logic. In this lesson, you will learn how to identify each type and how they relate.

Guided Path
1
Define general possible
The general possible (al-mumkin al-'amm) is that which has the negation of necessity in one of the two sides (existence or non-existence). In Razi's words: 'الممكن العام هو الذي يصدق عليه عدم الضرورة في أحد طرفي الوجود أو العدم'.
2
Define specific possible
The specific possible (al-mumkin al-khass) is that which has the negation of necessity on both sides together: 'الممكن الخاص هو الذي يصدق عدم الضرورة على طرفيه معًا'.
3
Understand the relationship
Razi explains that the general possible is true of both sides of the specific possible. This means that the specific possible is a subset of the general possible. For instance, a specific possible (like a contingent object) has no necessity on both sides, so it also satisfies the condition of having no necessity on at least one side.
4
Place the necessary and impossible
The necessary (wajib) is outside the general possible because it has necessity on both sides (necessity of existence and impossibility of non-existence). The impossible (mumtani') falls under the general possible because it has negation of necessity on the existence side (its non-existence is necessary, so no necessity of existence). So the division is three: necessary, possible (general), impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول صراحة: [ واعلم أنَّ في لفظ الشَّيخ هي هنا نوع إشكال؛ لأنَّه قال: الإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، فقد خصَّص هذا الإمكان بما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وليس كذلك؛ فإن هذا الإمكان حاصل أيضًا في سلب ضرورة الوجود، ألا ترى أنَّ إمكان العدم بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص؛ لأن قولك يمكن أن لا يكون بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص، فالإمكان إذن أعم من الإمكان في الوجود أو الإمكان في العدم، والذي يلزم سلب ضرورة العدم هو إمكان الوجود لا الإمكان المطلق، فكأنَّه فسَّر العام بالخاص. فالواجب أن يقال: الإمكان ما يلازم سلب الضَّرورة في أحد جانبي الوجود والعدم، فإن كان في جانب الوجود فهو الذي يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وإنْ كان في جانب العدم فهو الذى يلازم سلب ضرورة الوجود. قال: وإما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود إلى آخره؛ أقول: قد عرفت أن الممكن العام هو الذي يصدق عليه عدم الضَّرورة في أحد طرفي الوجود أو العدم، والممكن الخاص هو الذى يصدق عدم الضّرورة على طرفيه معًا، فحينئذ يكون الممكن العام صادقًا على طرفي الممكن الخاص، فلأجل ذلك نقلوا اسم الممكن إليه من المعنى الأوّل والواجب خارج عن هذا الممكن والقسمة بحسبه ثلاثة: إما واجبة، أو ممكنة، أو ممتنعة. ولفظ الكتاب هي هنا أيضًا مشعر بأنَّ الإمكان الخاص ليس هو نفس سلب الضّرورتين، بل هو أمر يلازم ذلك السلب، ونحن قد بيّنا فى كتابنا الكبير أنَّه يمتنع أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيّا. وهذا البحث وإن كان غير لائق بالمنطق لكنا نحتاج إليه لاستخراج فوائد ألفاظ الكتاب.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
8 min
Does Conception Entail Assent?
Discusses whether tasawwur entails tasdiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the relationship between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) using al-Razi's distinction between nominal and real definitions.

Core Idea
Nominal definitions precede assent; real definitions require it.

This lesson explores a central epistemological question in Islamic logic: when we have a judgment, are its component concepts automatically known? Al-Razi's answer depends on the type of definition involved. We will unpack his reasoning and see how this distinction resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage asks: Does conception (tasawwur) entail assent (tasdiq)? In other words, if you know a judgment (e.g., water boils at 100°C), must you also know the essences of its parts (water, boiling, degree)?
This is not a trivial question—it affects how we understand the process of acquiring knowledge.
2
Analyze the example
Consider the judgment 'water boils at 100 degrees.' To know this, do you need to have a complete conception of water, boiling, and degree? The passage suggests that nominal knowledge may suffice for understanding the judgment, but real knowledge requires deeper assent.
Al-Razi uses this example to motivate the need for two types of definition.
3
Unpack al-Razi's solution
Al-Razi introduces two types of definition: hadd bil-ism (definition by name) and hadd bil-haqiqa (definition by reality). The first is prior to assent, the second includes assent. This resolves the puzzle: the relation between conception and assent depends on the type of definition involved.
Definition by name merely clarifies meaning; definition by reality affirms existence or composition.
4
Apply to a new example
Take the term 'riba' in fiqh. A nominal definition ('increase without compensation') does not require assent to its prohibition. But a real definition ('excess in a barter transaction of specific goods') requires assent to its essence and legal effect.
This shows how the distinction operates in Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - المبحث الأكثر جدلاً حول محتوى العمل هي قضية: أن التصور هل يتضمن التصديق أم لا. وبعبارة أخرى، عندما يكون الحكم معلوما، فهل تكون أجزاؤه معلومة أم لا؟ مثال ذلك: هل يجب على الشخص ليكون عالما بأن غليان الماء عند ۱۰۰ درجة أن يعرف معه ماهية المحتوى الماء والغليان أو الدرجة؟ ولقد توسع الرازي في مناقشة هذه المسألة في شرح الإشارات، وذكر أن هناك نوعين من الحد: الحد بالاسم والحد بالحقيقة. وبالنسبة للرازي، فإن الحد بالاسم يكون قبل التصديق، والحد بالحقيقة يأتي بعد التصديق؛ أي أنه يتضمن التصديق. فعلى سبيل المثال: لا يتطلب تعريف الإنسان الناطق موافقة، ولكن في تعريف أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق أي عاقل، فإن تعريف الحيوان يتطلب تصديقًا. ونستنتج بذلك، أنه لا يمكننا تقبل أن مبحث مناقشة العلاقة بين التصور والتصديق ظهر فيما بعد الرازي.
C - The most controversial topic regarding the content of the work is the issue: Does conception entail assent or not? In other words, when a judgment is known, are its parts known or not? For example: must a person, in order to know that water boils at 100 degrees, also know with it the quiddity of the content water and boiling or degree? Al-Razi expanded on this issue in his commentary on al-Isharat, and mentioned that there are two types of definition: definition by name and definition by reality. According to al-Razi, definition by name comes before assent, and definition by reality comes after assent; that is, it includes assent. For example: the definition of 'rational man' does not require agreement, but in the definition that man is a rational animal, i.e., intelligent, the definition of 'animal' requires assent. We conclude from that that we cannot accept that the discussion of the relationship between conception and assent appeared after al-Razi.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the relationship between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) in the context of definitions. It begins by posing the central question: when we have a judgment (hukm), are its component concepts automatically known? The example given is knowing that water boils at 100 degrees — does one need to know the essence of water, boiling, and degrees? This sets up the problem. The author then cites al-Razi's treatment in his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat, where al-Razi distinguishes two types of definition: hadd bil-ism (definition by name) and hadd bil-haqiqa (definition by reality). According to al-Razi, definition by name is prior to and does not require assent (tasdiq) — it simply gives a nominal understanding. Definition by reality, however, comes after assent and involves it, meaning that to define something by its real essence requires prior assent that the thing exists or has that essence. The example clarifies: defining 'man' as 'rational animal' (hadd bil-haqiqa) requires agreeing that 'animal' is a correct genus, which is an act of assent. The conclusion is that this debate existed before al-Razi and was not invented later.

The relationship between conception and assent is clarified by distinguishing nominal definitions (which precede assent) from real definitions (which include it).

Al-Razi's Two Types of Definition: Bil-Ism and Bil-Haqiqa
Distinguishes two types of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition by name (hadd bil-ism) and definition by reality (hadd bil-haqiqa) and apply this distinction to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Nominal definitions clarify meaning; real definitions reveal essence and require assent.

Al-Razi's taxonomy of definitions into two types—by name and by reality—is a key contribution to Islamic logic. This lesson breaks down each type, their epistemological order, and how they function in defining terms. The distinction is especially useful in fields like usul al-fiqh where jurists must know when a definition is merely lexical versus when it carries ontological or legal commitment.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types
Al-Razi identifies two types of definition: hadd bil-ism (definition by name) and hadd bil-haqiqa (definition by reality). The first is a nominal definition that gives the meaning of a word; the second is a real definition that describes the essence of a thing.
This is not found in earlier logicians like Ibn Sina in this explicit form.
2
Identify their epistemological order
According to al-Razi, definition by name comes before assent (tasdiq), while definition by reality comes after assent and includes it. This means you can know the meaning of a word without assenting to the existence of its referent, but real definition requires you to affirm that the essence is correctly captured.
Example: 'unicorn' has a nominal definition but no real definition because unicorns do not exist.
3
Analyze the given example
The passage gives the example of 'rational man'. The definition 'rational man' can be taken as a nominal definition—it simply explains what the phrase means. But if we define 'man' as 'rational animal', we are giving a real definition, and defining 'animal' requires assent to the genus.
This shows that real definitions are compound and involve components that themselves require justification.
4
Apply to a new term
Take the term 'salat' (prayer). A nominal definition: 'a set of specific words and actions'. A real definition: 'a worship act comprising recitation, bowing, and prostration, performed with intention, that forbids indecency and evil'. The latter requires assent that this essence is correct.
In fiqh, the real definition of salat is disputed among madhabs, showing the importance of this distinction.

Necessary and Acquired Knowledge: The Foundational Distinction
Author states that knowledge is self-evident and divides it into necessary (badihi) and acquired (kasbi).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (badihi) and acquired (kasbi) knowledge by identifying their defining characteristics and providing original examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Knowledge is self-evident; its division into necessary and acquired is the bedrock of Islamic epistemology.

This passage opens a chapter on the nature of knowledge. The author makes a bold claim: the concept of knowledge itself is so primitive that it needs no definition. He then introduces a critical distinction that shapes all subsequent Islamic thought: some knowledge comes to us without effort (necessary), while other knowledge requires reflection and proof (acquired). Understanding this division is the first step in mastering how Islamic scholars reason from self-evident truths to derived conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence where the author says knowledge needs no definition. Notice the reason given: it belongs to primary notions (badihiyyat).
The Arabic phrase 'غني عن التعريف' means it is independent of definition.
2
Extract the two types of knowledge
Locate the two categories the author mentions: badihi (necessary) and kasbi (acquired). Note the key contrast: one requires no acquisition in conception, the other does.
The word 'الكسب' means acquisition or effort.
3
Examine the examples provided
Look at the two examples: 'the whole is greater than its part' (necessary) and 'the whole as such is not identical to each one of its parts' (acquired). Why is the first immediate and the second not?
The first is an axiom; the second requires understanding the relationship between a whole and its parts.
4
Analyze the common and distinguishing elements
The author notes that for two things to be different, they share something (here, the concept of knowledge) and differ by something else. Identify what is common and what distinguishes necessary from acquired knowledge.
Both are cases of knowledge; they differ in how they are attained.
5
Apply to a new Islamic example
Choose a necessary truth from kalam (e.g., a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent at the same time) and an acquired truth (e.g., the existence of the Creator from the existence of the world). Confirm that the first is immediate and the second requires reasoning.
This reinforces the distinction and shows its utility in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العلم، فإنه من حيث هو هو غني عن التعريف؛ لافتقار ما هو من البديهيات إليه وقد كان من العلوم ما يكون بديهيا لا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب أصلا؛ نحو علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه. ومنها ما يكون كسبيا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب؛ مثل علمنا بأن الكل من حيث هو الكل غير كل واحد مثلا. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إلا وأن يكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على ما به المشاركة؛ وهو نفس العلم وعلى ما به المغايرة؛ وهو غيره وإلا
As for knowledge, in itself it is needless of definition, because it belongs to primary notions. Some knowledge is intuitive, needing no acquisition in conception, like our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part. And some is acquired, needing acquisition in conception, like our knowledge that the whole as such is not identical to each one of its parts. And this can only be so if each of the two [conceptions] includes something in common and something distinct.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the nature of knowledge. The author first asserts that the concept of knowledge itself is self-evident and does not require a definition because it is a primary notion (badihi). Then he distinguishes two types of knowledge: intuitive (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). Intuitive knowledge is immediate, requiring no mental effort, exemplified by the axiom 'the whole is greater than its part.' Acquired knowledge requires reasoning, exemplified by the proposition 'the whole as such is not identical to each of its parts.' He then remarks that for two things to be different, they must share a common element (here, the concept of knowledge) and have a differentiating element. This is a foundational epistemological distinction in Islamic logic. In Islamic sciences, necessary knowledge includes knowledge of self-evident truths like the impossibility of a thing being both existent and non-existent simultaneously; acquired knowledge includes truths derived through syllogisms, such as the existence of the Creator from the existence of the world.

Key Terms

البديهي
الكسبي

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a self-evident concept, but it manifests in two forms: necessary (immediate) and acquired (requiring reasoning), and this distinction underpins all Islamic reasoning.

Why Knowledge is Self-Evident
Author argues knowledge is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's argument that knowledge must be self-evident based on the shared nature of self-evident and acquired knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is self-evident because it is the common element in all knowledge.

The author begins by asserting that knowledge, in its essence, does not need a definition because it is a primary concept. To support this, he distinguishes between two types of knowledge: self-evident (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). This lesson unpacks that distinction and the reasoning that leads to the conclusion that knowledge itself is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of knowledge
Look for the terms 'badihi' (self-evident) and 'kasbi' (acquired) in the Arabic. The author divides knowledge into these two types.
2
Note the examples of each type
The self-evident example is 'the whole is greater than its part'; the acquired example is 'the whole as such is not any single part'. Recognise that the second requires reasoning.
3
Understand the shared nature of knowledge
Both types must share the essence of knowledge, otherwise they could not be compared or distinguished. This shows that knowledge as such is common to both.
4
Reason from self-evidence to independence from definition
Since the self-evident type does not need acquisition, and knowledge as such is the self-evident element, knowledge itself is self-evident and therefore does not require a definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في العلم ما هو؟ ]
أما العلم، فإنه من حيث هو هو غني عن التعريف؛ لافتقار ما هو من البديهيات إليه وقد كان من العلوم ما يكون بديهيا لا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب أصلا ؛ نحو علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه. ومنها ما يكون كسبيا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب؛ مثل علمنا بأن الكل من حيث هو الكل غير كل واحد مثلا. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إلا وأن يكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على ما به المشاركة؛ وهو نفس العلم وعلى ما به المغايرة؛ وهو غيره وإلا لا يكون متميزا عن الغير فيكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى العلم من حيث هو العلم لا محالة. والبديهي منهما لا يفتقر إلى الشيء إلا وأن يكون ذلك الشيء بديهيا؛ فالعلم إذن من حيث هو العلم بديهي؛ والبديهي لا يفتقر إلى الكسب البتة وإلا لا يكون بديهيا فكذلك العلم. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون غنيا عن التعريف؛ إذ التعريف عبارة عن إثبات معرفة الشيء، وإثبات المعرفة بعد ثبوتها محال.
وقد يقال فيه: تعريف ماهية الشيء لا يمكن إلا لشيء كانت معرفة ذلك الشيء سابقة على معرفة ما هو المقصود معرفته من بعد. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن تعريف ماهية العلم من حيث هو العلم. فإن ما يعرف به العلم لا يخلو من أن يكون من البديهيات - والبديهيات كلها في المعرفة على السواء- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء بما يساويه في المعرفة؛ وذلك محال، أو لا يكون من البديهيات - وغير البديهي أخفى من البديهي لا محالة- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء مما هو الأخفى منه في المعرفة؛ وإنه محال كذلك. وقد يقال أيضًا : تعريفُ الشيء لا يمكن إلا بعد العلم بماهية التعريف وكيفيته. ولو كان كذلك لكان تعريف العلم من جملة المستحيلات؛ لما أنه لا يمكن إلا بعد حصوله؛ وتحصيل الشيء بعد حصوله محال . ولئن قيل : كيف هو وقد كان من التفات من يعرف العلم بأنه عبارة عن تبيين الشيء كما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عن معرفة الشيء على ما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عما يوجب كون المحل عالمًا بالشيء، وعلى هذا فإن فيه من التعريفات المختلفة، فنقول : هذه التعريفات وما هو بمثلها فيما نحن فيه باطل . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المقصود من هذه التعريفات تعريف معنى اللفظ لا تعريف ماهية العلم؛ وحينئذ يصحُ البعض من هذه التعريفات لا كلها.
Chapter [On what is knowledge?]
As for knowledge, from the perspective of its essence, it is independent of definition; because what is self-evident does not need it. And among the [items of] knowledge, there is that which is self-evident, not needing acquisition in conception at all, like our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part. And among them is that which is acquired, needing acquisition in conception, like our knowledge that the whole as such is not any single one, for example. And it cannot be thus unless each of them includes that by which they share, which is knowledge itself, and that by which they differ, which is other than it; otherwise it would not be distinguished from the other, so each of them would necessarily depend on knowledge as such. And the self-evident among them does not depend on anything unless that thing is self-evident. So knowledge, from the perspective of its essence, is self-evident. And the self-evident does not need acquisition at all, otherwise it would not be self-evident; likewise knowledge. Then it follows that it is independent of definition, for definition is the establishment of knowledge of a thing, and establishing knowledge after it has been established is impossible.
And it may be said concerning it: Definition of the essence of a thing is only possible for something whose knowledge precedes the knowledge of what is intended to be known afterwards. And if that is the case, then it is impossible to define the essence of knowledge from the perspective of its essence. For that by which knowledge is defined is either from among the self-evident things — and all self-evident things are equal in knowledge — so defining it by that is defining a thing by what equals it in knowledge, and that is impossible; or it is not from among the self-evident things — and the non-self-evident is necessarily more obscure than the self-evident — so defining it by that is defining a thing by what is more obscure than it in knowledge, and that is also impossible. And it may also be said: Definition of a thing is not possible except after knowledge of the essence of definition and its modality. And if that is the case, then defining knowledge would be among the impossibilities, because it cannot happen except after its own occurrence, and acquiring a thing after it has already occurred is impossible. And if it is said: How so, when there have been those who define knowledge as 'clarifying a thing as it is,' and others define it as 'knowing a thing as it is,' and others define it as 'that which causes the subject to be knowing of the thing,' and on this [basis] there are various definitions? Then we say: These definitions and the like are invalid in what we are discussing. Unless it is said that the purpose of these definitions is to define the meaning of the word, not to define the essence of knowledge; in that case, some of these definitions are valid, not all.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument that knowledge (al-'ilm) is self-evident and cannot be defined. The author begins by stating that knowledge as such is independent of definition because it is a primary concept (badihi). He distinguishes between self-evident knowledge (e.g., 'the whole is greater than its part') and acquired knowledge (e.g., 'the whole as such is not any single part'). He then argues that both types share the nature of knowledge and are distinguished by their objects; if they were not both knowledge, they could not be compared. Since the self-evident type does not require acquisition, and knowledge as such is self-evident, it needs no definition. A definition would require prior knowledge of the defined thing, which is circular.

The author then presents two additional arguments against defining knowledge:
1) A definition must be clearer than the defined thing. But knowledge is self-evident, so anything used to define it is either equally clear (resulting in a definition that is no better) or more obscure (which would not clarify). Both are impossible.
2) To define knowledge, one must already know what definition is, but that requires knowledge of knowledge itself, leading to an infinite regress.

Finally, the author addresses existing definitions of knowledge (e.g., 'clarifying a thing as it is' or 'knowing a thing as it is') and declares them invalid if taken as definitions of the essence of knowledge. However, he allows that some may be valid as definitions of the word (ma'na al-lafz) — that is, clarifying the conventional meaning of the term 'knowledge' without claiming to define its essence.

This is a standard Islamic epistemological position: self-evident concepts like 'knowledge' and 'existence' are undefinable. The argument guards against the fallacy of attempting to define the indefinable.

Key Terms
العلم
البديهي
الكسب
التعريف
ماهية الشيء
معنى اللفظ
الكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the author, why is knowledge independent of definition?
A) Because it is a complex concept too vast to be defined.
B) Because it is self-evident and any attempt to define it would be circular or regressive.
C) Because the Quran defines it as clear.
D) Because all definitions are pointless in Islamic thought.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge itself is self-evident and does not require definition because it is the common nature in all cognition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Impossibility of Defining Knowledge
Author refutes attempts to define knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three arguments against defining knowledge and evaluate why proposed definitions fail as definitions of essence.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge is impossible because any definition would either be circular, regressive, or fail to clarify.

After establishing that knowledge is self-evident, the author presents three additional arguments demonstrating that definition of knowledge is impossible. He also addresses and refutes common definitions found in philosophical discourse. This lesson analyzes these arguments and the crucial distinction between defining the essence and defining the word.

Guided Path
1
List the three arguments
Identify the three distinct arguments introduced after 'وقد يقال فيه' (and it may be said concerning it). They are: (a) defining knowledge by something equally self-evident or more obscure; (b) requiring prior knowledge of definition; (c) requiring knowledge itself before definition.
2
Analyze the logical problem in each argument
For (a): if the definiens is self-evident, it equals the definiendum in clarity, so no improvement; if not, it's more obscure, so no clarification. For (b): to define knowledge, you must know what definition is, which itself requires knowledge, leading to circularity. For (c): definition of a thing requires prior knowledge of it, but knowledge is what we are trying to define, making it impossible.
3
Recognize the refutation of existing definitions
The author then addresses counterexamples: definitions like 'knowledge is clarifying a thing as it is'. He declares them invalid as definitions of the essence (mahiyat al-shay'). Understand why they fail: they are either circular or do not identify the essence.
4
Note the concession for nominal definitions
The author allows that these definitions may be valid as definitions of the word (ma'na al-lafz), i.e., explaining the conventional meaning of the term 'knowledge' without claiming to define its essence.
This distinction is crucial in later usul al-fiqh and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في العلم ما هو؟ ]
أما العلم، فإنه من حيث هو هو غني عن التعريف؛ لافتقار ما هو من البديهيات إليه وقد كان من العلوم ما يكون بديهيا لا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب أصلا ؛ نحو علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه. ومنها ما يكون كسبيا يفتقر في التصور إلى الكسب؛ مثل علمنا بأن الكل من حيث هو الكل غير كل واحد مثلا. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إلا وأن يكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على ما به المشاركة؛ وهو نفس العلم وعلى ما به المغايرة؛ وهو غيره وإلا لا يكون متميزا عن الغير فيكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى العلم من حيث هو العلم لا محالة. والبديهي منهما لا يفتقر إلى الشيء إلا وأن يكون ذلك الشيء بديهيا؛ فالعلم إذن من حيث هو العلم بديهي؛ والبديهي لا يفتقر إلى الكسب البتة وإلا لا يكون بديهيا فكذلك العلم. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون غنيا عن التعريف؛ إذ التعريف عبارة عن إثبات معرفة الشيء، وإثبات المعرفة بعد ثبوتها محال.
وقد يقال فيه: تعريف ماهية الشيء لا يمكن إلا لشيء كانت معرفة ذلك الشيء سابقة على معرفة ما هو المقصود معرفته من بعد. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن تعريف ماهية العلم من حيث هو العلم. فإن ما يعرف به العلم لا يخلو من أن يكون من البديهيات - والبديهيات كلها في المعرفة على السواء- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء بما يساويه في المعرفة؛ وذلك محال، أو لا يكون من البديهيات - وغير البديهي أخفى من البديهي لا محالة- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء مما هو الأخفى منه في المعرفة؛ وإنه محال كذلك. وقد يقال أيضًا : تعريفُ الشيء لا يمكن إلا بعد العلم بماهية التعريف وكيفيته. ولو كان كذلك لكان تعريف العلم من جملة المستحيلات؛ لما أنه لا يمكن إلا بعد حصوله؛ وتحصيل الشيء بعد حصوله محال . ولئن قيل : كيف هو وقد كان من التفات من يعرف العلم بأنه عبارة عن تبيين الشيء كما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عن معرفة الشيء على ما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عما يوجب كون المحل عالمًا بالشيء، وعلى هذا فإن فيه من التعريفات المختلفة، فنقول : هذه التعريفات وما هو بمثلها فيما نحن فيه باطل . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المقصود من هذه التعريفات تعريف معنى اللفظ لا تعريف ماهية العلم؛ وحينئذ يصحُ البعض من هذه التعريفات لا كلها.
Chapter [On what is knowledge?]
As for knowledge, from the perspective of its essence, it is independent of definition; because what is self-evident does not need it. And among the [items of] knowledge, there is that which is self-evident, not needing acquisition in conception at all, like our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part. And among them is that which is acquired, needing acquisition in conception, like our knowledge that the whole as such is not any single one, for example. And it cannot be thus unless each of them includes that by which they share, which is knowledge itself, and that by which they differ, which is other than it; otherwise it would not be distinguished from the other, so each of them would necessarily depend on knowledge as such. And the self-evident among them does not depend on anything unless that thing is self-evident. So knowledge, from the perspective of its essence, is self-evident. And the self-evident does not need acquisition at all, otherwise it would not be self-evident; likewise knowledge. Then it follows that it is independent of definition, for definition is the establishment of knowledge of a thing, and establishing knowledge after it has been established is impossible.
And it may be said concerning it: Definition of the essence of a thing is only possible for something whose knowledge precedes the knowledge of what is intended to be known afterwards. And if that is the case, then it is impossible to define the essence of knowledge from the perspective of its essence. For that by which knowledge is defined is either from among the self-evident things — and all self-evident things are equal in knowledge — so defining it by that is defining a thing by what equals it in knowledge, and that is impossible; or it is not from among the self-evident things — and the non-self-evident is necessarily more obscure than the self-evident — so defining it by that is defining a thing by what is more obscure than it in knowledge, and that is also impossible. And it may also be said: Definition of a thing is not possible except after knowledge of the essence of definition and its modality. And if that is the case, then defining knowledge would be among the impossibilities, because it cannot happen except after its own occurrence, and acquiring a thing after it has already occurred is impossible. And if it is said: How so, when there have been those who define knowledge as 'clarifying a thing as it is,' and others define it as 'knowing a thing as it is,' and others define it as 'that which causes the subject to be knowing of the thing,' and on this [basis] there are various definitions? Then we say: These definitions and the like are invalid in what we are discussing. Unless it is said that the purpose of these definitions is to define the meaning of the word, not to define the essence of knowledge; in that case, some of these definitions are valid, not all.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument that knowledge (al-'ilm) is self-evident and cannot be defined. The author begins by stating that knowledge as such is independent of definition because it is a primary concept (badihi). He distinguishes between self-evident knowledge (e.g., 'the whole is greater than its part') and acquired knowledge (e.g., 'the whole as such is not any single part'). He then argues that both types share the nature of knowledge and are distinguished by their objects; if they were not both knowledge, they could not be compared. Since the self-evident type does not require acquisition, and knowledge as such is self-evident, it needs no definition. A definition would require prior knowledge of the defined thing, which is circular.

The author then presents two additional arguments against defining knowledge:
1) A definition must be clearer than the defined thing. But knowledge is self-evident, so anything used to define it is either equally clear (resulting in a definition that is no better) or more obscure (which would not clarify). Both are impossible.
2) To define knowledge, one must already know what definition is, but that requires knowledge of knowledge itself, leading to an infinite regress.

Finally, the author addresses existing definitions of knowledge (e.g., 'clarifying a thing as it is' or 'knowing a thing as it is') and declares them invalid if taken as definitions of the essence of knowledge. However, he allows that some may be valid as definitions of the word (ma'na al-lafz) — that is, clarifying the conventional meaning of the term 'knowledge' without claiming to define its essence.

This is a standard Islamic epistemological position: self-evident concepts like 'knowledge' and 'existence' are undefinable. The argument guards against the fallacy of attempting to define the indefinable.

Key Terms
العلم
البديهي
الكسب
التعريف
ماهية الشيء
معنى اللفظ
الكل

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is defining knowledge impossible?
A) Because definitions are always circular in Islamic thought.
B) Because to define knowledge, one must already know what definition is, which depends on knowledge, leading to an infinite regress.
C) Because knowledge is a simple concept that cannot be broken into genus and differentia.
D) Because the only valid definitions come from revelation.

Key Takeaway
Defining the essence of knowledge is logically impossible; only nominal definitions (ta'rif ma'na al-lafz) are permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Take one of the definitions of knowledge mentioned in the passage (e.g., 'knowledge is clarifying a thing as it is') and explain why it fails as a definition of essence.
?

Why knowledge cannot be defined
Author argues that knowledge as such is self-evident and thus indefinable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a self-evident concept cannot be defined, using the argument from the passage.

Core Idea
Self-evident knowledge cannot be defined because definition would prove what is already known.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental question: can knowledge itself be defined? He argues that knowledge, considered in its essence, is self-evident. This means it is already present to the mind without any need for proof. Building on this, he shows that any attempt to define it would be an impossible effort to prove what is already known. This lesson will walk you through the logical steps of this argument and its implications for the study of logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about knowledge
The author states that knowledge (al-'ilm) considered as such is self-evident (badihi). This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Understand what self-evident means
A self-evident concept requires no acquisition (kash) or proof; it is known by itself. This is a key premise.
Self-evident concepts are foundational in logic.
3
Connect self-evidence to indefinability
The author argues that if knowledge is self-evident, then any definition would be an attempt to 'affirm knowledge of a thing' after it is already known, which is impossible (muhal).
4
Evaluate the logical necessity
The conclusion follows necessarily: knowledge is independent of definition (ghaniyyan 'an al-ta'rif). This shows that not every concept needs a definition.
This guards against infinite regress in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكون متميزا عن الغير فيكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى العلم من حيث هو العلم لا محالة. والبديهي منهما لا يفتقر إلى الشيء إلا وأن يكون ذلك الشيء بديهيا؛ فالعلم إذن من حيث هو العلم بديهي؛ والبديهي لا يفتقر إلى الكسب البتة وإلا لا يكون بديهيا فكذلك العلم. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون غنيا عن التعريف؛ إذ التعريف عبارة عن إثبات معرفة الشيء، وإثبات المعرفة بعد ثبوتها محال.
It cannot be distinguished from others, so each of them is in need of knowledge, as such, without doubt. And the self-evident of them does not need anything except that thing be self-evident; so knowledge, then, as such is self-evident; and the self-evident does not require acquisition at all, otherwise it would not be self-evident, so too knowledge. Therefore, it follows that it is independent of definition; for definition is the affirmation of knowledge of a thing, and affirming knowledge after it is already established is impossible.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument about the nature of knowledge. The author first establishes that knowledge, considered in itself, is self-evident (badihi). Then he argues that what is self-evident does not require any acquisition (kash), and therefore knowledge cannot be defined. The reasoning is: if knowledge were definable, the definition would constitute a proof of knowledge, but since knowledge is already present, proving it again is impossible. This argument guards against the misconception that all concepts require definition; some are foundational and self-evident.

Key Terms

البديهي
التعريف

C) Because all definitions are circular
D) Because knowledge is a simple concept that cannot be broken down

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that self-evident concepts like knowledge are beyond definition, because defining them would be an attempt to prove what is already known.

The Impossibility of Defining Essence: The Epistemic Parity Argument
Author argues that defining the essence of knowledge is impossible due to epistemic parity or deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dilemma argument against defining the essence of knowledge and evaluate its two horns.

Core Idea
If a definition must be better known, then foundational concepts like knowledge cannot be defined.

This passage presents a classic epistemological objection to the project of defining essences, particularly for concepts like 'knowledge'. The author argues that any definition of knowledge either presupposes what it tries to explain or leads to deeper obscurity. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to spot circular or regressive definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined in the argument. The passage explicitly targets 'the essence of knowledge' (māhiyyat al-‘ilm).
The definiendum is the concept whose definition is being evaluated.
2
Extract the underlying condition for definitions
The argument assumes that a valid definition must be better known (ashhar) than the definiendum. This is a key epistemological principle.
If this condition is not accepted, the argument loses force.
3
Explore the first horn: self-evident definiens
If the definiens is from the self-evidents (badīhiyyāt), it is equally known as the definiendum (since all self-evidents are equal in knowledge). Thus the definition defines a thing by what is equal to it, which is circular and invalid.
Self-evidents are foundational and cannot be defined by simpler concepts.
4
Explore the second horn: non-self-evident definiens
If the definiens is not self-evident, it is necessarily more hidden (akhfā) than the self-evident definiendum. Defining by something more obscure makes the definition worse, which is also invalid.
Non-self-evident terms depend on self-evident ones for their clarity.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Since both options lead to an impossible definition, the essence of knowledge cannot be defined at all.
This is a constructive dilemma: either case results in failure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال فيه: تعريف ماهية الشيء لا يمكن إلا لشيء كانت معرفة ذلك الشيء شايعة على معرفة ما هو المقصود معرفته من بعد. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن تعريف ماهية العلم من حيث هو العلم. فإن ما يعرف به العلم لا يخلو من أن يكون من البديهيات - والبديهيات كلها في المعرفة على السواء- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء بما يساويه في المعرفة؛ وذلك محال، أو لا يكون من البديهيات - وغير البديهي أخفى من البديهي لا محالة- فيكون تعريفه بذلك تعريف الشيء مما هو الأخفى منه في المعرفة؛ وإنه محال كذلك.
And it may be said therein: defining the essence of a thing is only possible for something whose knowledge of that thing is more common than the knowledge of what is intended to be known afterwards. If so, then it is impossible to define the essence of knowledge as such. For that by which knowledge is defined is either from the self-evidents – and all self-evidents are equal in knowledge – so defining it by that would be defining a thing by what is equal to it in knowledge; and that is impossible; or it is not from the self-evidents – and the non-self-evident is necessarily more hidden than the self-evident – so defining it by that would be defining a thing by what is more hidden than it in knowledge; and that is likewise impossible.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against defining the essence of knowledge. The argument assumes that a valid definition must be better known than the definiendum. The author examines two possibilities: either the definiens is self-evident, in which case the definition is circular because it explains something with something equally known; or the definiens is not self-evident, in which case it is more obscure, leading to a worse understanding. Both cases are impossible, so defining the essence of knowledge is impossible. This argument is common in critiques of defining foundational concepts.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya)
البديهيات (al-badīhiyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It becomes more obscure
C) It becomes equally clear
D) It becomes impossible to understand

Key Takeaway
The essence of foundational concepts like knowledge cannot be defined because any definition either equals or worsens the epistemic status.

The Circularity Problem in Defining Knowledge
Author argues defining knowledge is impossible because it requires prior knowledge of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the circularity argument against defining knowledge and identify its logical structure.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge presupposes definition, which is itself knowledge — a vicious circle.

How can we define 'knowledge' if the very act of defining requires that we already know what a definition is? This lesson unpacks a classic skeptical argument that challenges the possibility of defining foundational concepts. We will analyze the logical structure of this argument and see why it threatens the project of defining 'ilm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if-then' statement in the Arabic: 'defining a thing is only possible after knowledge of the essence of definition and its modality.' This is the key conditional that sets up the problem.
The conditional is expressed as 'لا يمكن إلا بعد' (only possible after).
2
Apply the conditional to defining knowledge
Take the special case where the thing being defined is knowledge itself. According to the conditional, defining knowledge requires knowing the essence of definition. But that knowledge is itself knowledge, so we already need to have knowledge to define knowledge.
This creates a circular dependency: defining knowledge presupposes knowledge of definition, which is a type of knowledge.
3
Recognize the impossibility conclusion
The author concludes that defining knowledge is impossible because it would require knowledge that is already possessed before the definition is complete. This is like trying to lift yourself by your own bootstraps.
The argument uses the principle that attaining something after it is already attained is impossible (تحصيل الحاصل محال).
4
Evaluate the argument's force
Consider whether this argument succeeds in proving that defining knowledge is impossible. Could the definition of definition itself be given without circularity? This step encourages critical thinking about the limits of definition.
This argument is a classic skeptical move; later logicians responded by distinguishing between innate knowledge and acquired definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال أيضًا : تعريفُ الشيء لا يمكن إلا بعد العلم بماهية التعريف وكيفيته. ولو كان كذلك لكان تعريف العلم من جملة المستحيلات؛ لما أنه لا يمكن إلا بعد حصوله؛ وتحصيل الشيء بعد حصوله محال.
And it may also be said: defining a thing is only possible after knowledge of the essence of definition and its modality. If so, then defining knowledge would be among the impossibilities; because it is only possible after its attainment; and attaining a thing after its attainment is impossible.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This third argument identifies a circularity problem: to define 'ilm (knowledge), one must first know what a definition is and how it works. But that knowledge itself is a kind of 'ilm, so defining knowledge requires knowledge of definition, which itself is a form of knowledge. This leads to an infinite regress or a circularity. Therefore, defining knowledge is impossible because it would require the very thing it seeks to establish. This argument is a classic skeptical move against the possibility of defining foundational concepts.

Key Terms
ماهية التعريف (māhiyyat al-ta'rīf): The essence or nature of definition; what definition is in itself. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main objection raised in this argument?
A) That definition is impossible because all definitions are circular
B) That defining knowledge requires prior knowledge of definition, creating a circular dependency
C) That knowledge is too simple to be defined
D) That definition requires external evidence

Key Takeaway
The attempt to define knowledge is threatened by circularity because defining any concept presupposes a definition of definition, which is itself knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Division of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author defines knowledge and divides it into conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tasawwur and tasdiq using the criteria from the source, and classify any given item of knowledge as one or the other.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare concept or a judgment: conception grasps an essence, assent decides truth.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of knowledge given by the author, separating it into conception and assent. Understanding this division is crucial for all subsequent logical analysis, as it determines how we handle definitions versus propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the author's definition of knowledge and its two subdivisions. Find the exact Arabic phrases for tasawwur and tasdiq.
2
Define tasawwur
Extract the definition: 'idrak mahiyyat al-shay' min haythu hiya al-mahiyyah'. Understand that it is apprehension without judgment.
3
Define tasdiq
Extract the definition: 'al-hukm alayha bi al-nafy aw bi al-ithbat'. Note that it includes both affirmation and negation.
This is important for the next lesson.
4
Compare the two
Contrast tasawwur (mere conception) with tasdiq (judgment). Give a concrete example: understanding 'human' vs. judging 'Zayd is human'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في العلم والجهل وأقسامهما ] فالعلم من حيث هو العلم إما تصور؛ وهو إدراك ماهية الشيء من حيث هي الماهية، وإما تصديق ؛ وهو الحكم عليها بالنفي أو بالإثبات. ولا يظن أن الحكم بالنفي هو نفي الحكم؛ إذ الفرق بين بين نفي الحكم وبين الحكم بالنفي لما كان المنفي في نفي الحكم هو الحكم ، وفي الحكم بالنفي غيره وكذلك بين التصور والتصديق ؛ فإنه فرق ما بين الشيء وما يفتقر إليه الشيء
Section: On knowledge and ignorance and their divisions. Knowledge, as such, is either conception: which is the apprehension of the quiddity of a thing as it is quiddity; or assent: which is a judgment about it by negation or affirmation. And one should not think that judgment by negation is the negation of judgment; for the difference between the negation of judgment and judgment by negation is that what is negated in negation of judgment is the judgment itself, while in judgment by negation it is other than the judgment; and similarly between conception and assent; for it is the difference between a thing and that which a thing requires.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage provides the classic definition of knowledge in Islamic logic: it divides into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). Conception is the apprehension of the essence without judgment (e.g., understanding 'human' without affirming or denying anything). Assent is the judgment that something is or is not the case. The author warns against confusing 'judgment by negation' with 'negation of judgment'. The former is a negative proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not writing'), which is still an assent; the latter would be the absence of judgment entirely. The distinction is crucial for understanding propositional attitudes and the structure of knowledge.

Key Terms

التصديق

A) Judgment about a thing
B) Apprehension of the quiddity without judgment

Key Takeaway
Knowledge divides into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), and this division is foundational for all logical analysis.

Judgment by Negation vs. Negation of Judgment
Author clarifies the difference between judgment by negation and negation of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'judgment by negation' (al-hukm bi al-nafy) and 'negation of judgment' (nafy al-hukm) using the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Saying 'Zayd is not writing' is a judgment; saying nothing is not a judgment.

The author warns against a subtle confusion: thinking that a negative proposition is the same as having no judgment. This lesson explains the difference, which is essential for correctly identifying cases of tasdiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Find 'judgment by negation' (al-hukm bi al-nafy) and 'negation of judgment' (nafy al-hukm) in the Arabic text.
2
Understand the difference
The author explains: in 'negation of judgment', what is negated is the judgment itself; in 'judgment by negation', what is negated is something else (the predicate). So 'judgment by negation' is a type of judgment, while 'negation of judgment' is no judgment at all.
3
Apply to an example
Consider the statement 'Zayd is not writing'. This is a judgment by negation (a tasdiq). Silence or suspension of judgment would be negation of judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في العلم والجهل وأقسامهما ] فالعلم من حيث هو العلم إما تصور؛ وهو إدراك ماهية الشيء من حيث هي الماهية، وإما تصديق ؛ وهو الحكم عليها بالنفي أو بالإثبات. ولا يظن أن الحكم بالنفي هو نفي الحكم؛ إذ الفرق بين بين نفي الحكم وبين الحكم بالنفي لما كان المنفي في نفي الحكم هو الحكم ، وفي الحكم بالنفي غيره وكذلك بين التصور والتصديق ؛ فإنه فرق ما بين الشيء وما يفتقر إليه الشيء
Section: On knowledge and ignorance and their divisions. Knowledge, as such, is either conception: which is the apprehension of the quiddity of a thing as it is quiddity; or assent: which is a judgment about it by negation or affirmation. And one should not think that judgment by negation is the negation of judgment; for the difference between the negation of judgment and judgment by negation is that what is negated in negation of judgment is the judgment itself, while in judgment by negation it is other than the judgment; and similarly between conception and assent; for it is the difference between a thing and that which a thing requires.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage provides the classic definition of knowledge in Islamic logic: it divides into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). Conception is the apprehension of the essence without judgment (e.g., understanding 'human' without affirming or denying anything). Assent is the judgment that something is or is not the case. The author warns against confusing 'judgment by negation' with 'negation of judgment'. The former is a negative proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not writing'), which is still an assent; the latter would be the absence of judgment entirely. The distinction is crucial for understanding propositional attitudes and the structure of knowledge.

The Three Considerations of Essence (Mahiyya)
Author discusses the term 'ilm, essence, and its three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three considerations of essence (mahiyya) and explain why logical attributes like predication are mental accidents.

Core Idea
Essence can be considered in three ways: by itself, externally, and mentally; logical properties exist only in the mind.

In this passage, the author clarifies that the term 'ilm is not ambiguous, and that the concept of a term is separate from the essence it signifies. Then he introduces the three considerations of essence: as itself, as existing externally, and as existing in the mind. This distinction is crucial for understanding how logical attributes like being a subject or predicate are mental accidents, not real external properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations
Read the passage and list the three considerations of essence mentioned: (1) essence as it is, without any attachments; (2) essence as it exists externally; (3) essence as it exists in the mind.
The author numbers them with [1], [ب], [ت].
2
Understand essence by itself
The essence as such (الماهية بما هي هي) is more general than essences in external or mental existence. It has no accidental attributes at all.
3
Contrast external and mental existence
In external existence, essences have concrete instances but no logical attributes like subject/predicate or universality. In mental existence, essences acquire attributes like position (wad'), predication (haml), particularity, universality, etc. The author states that in external existents there is no essentiality or accidentally in predication.
Consider the example: the essence 'human' exists externally as Zayd (individual) and mentally as a universal concept that can be a subject in a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لفظ العلم لا يكون من الألفاظ المشتركة، ولا يظن أن مفهوم لفظ العلم وماهيته واحد. وقد كان مفهوم اللفظ من حيث مفهوم اللفظ قد يعلم بدون العلم بالماهية؛ وبالعكس كذلك فإن من الماهيات ما كان معلومًا للبعض من الأشخاص من غير أن يعلم بأن ما يدل عليه اللفظ ما هو؛ وهذا ظاهر. ثم من اللوازم أن يعلم بأن لكل شيء ماهية وماهية الشيء من حيث هي الماهية أعم بالنسبة إلى ماهية هي في الأعيان. وبالنسبة إلى ماهية هي في الأذهان كذلك. فيكون بها اعتبارات ثلاثة [1] اعتبار الماهية بما هي هي غير مضافة أصلا لا إلى الوجود الذهني ولا إلى الوجود الخارجي ولا إلى الغير مما يلحقها من حيث هي [ب] كذلك اعتبارها من حيث هي في الأعيان فيلحقها حينئذ من الأعراض ما يختص وجودها ذلك من حيث هي في الأعيان، [ت] واعتبارها من حيث هي في الأذهان فيلحقها حينئذ من الأعراض ما يختص وجودها ذلك مثل الوضع والحمل، ومثل الجزئية والكلية والذاتية والعرضية، وغير [۳] ذلك. فإنه ليس في الموجودات الخارجية ذاتية ولا عرضية في الحمل / فلا يكون البعض منها موضوعا ولا محمولا ولا مقدمة ولا قياسا ولا غير ذلك؛ وهذه كلها من جملة ما يعرف بالتأمل.
For the term 'ilm is not among ambiguous terms, nor should it be thought that the concept of the term 'ilm and its essence are one. The concept of the term, from the aspect of being a concept, can be known without knowledge of the essence, and vice versa; for among essences there are those known to some persons without their knowing what the term signifies. This is obvious. Then it is necessary to know that every thing has an essence, and the essence of a thing insofar as it is essence is more general than an essence that is in external things, and likewise for an essence in minds. Thus it has three considerations: [1] consideration of the essence as it is, without being added at all to either mental existence or external existence or to anything else that attaches to it as such; [b] likewise its consideration as it is in external things, whereupon there attach to it then the accidents specific to that existence as it is in external things; [t] and its consideration as it is in minds, whereupon there attach to it then the accidents specific to that existence, such as position and predication, and particularity and universality, essentiality and accidentally, and other than that. For in external existents there is no essentiality or accidentally in predication, so some of them are not subjects or predicates, premises or syllogisms, or anything else. And all of these are among things known by reflection.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a metaphysical and epistemological clarification of the term 'ilm (knowledge) and the concept of essence (mahiyya). The author begins by stating that 'ilm is not equivocal; the concept of the term 'ilm is distinct from the essence it signifies. One can know the concept without knowing the essence, and sometimes know an essence without knowing the term. This establishes a separation between language and reality. Then the author asserts that every thing has an essence, and the essence as such is more general than essences considered as externally existing or mentally existing. There are three considerations of essence: (1) the essence as it is alone, with no attachments to mental or external existence; (2) the essence as it exists externally, where it receives accidental properties appropriate to external existence; (3) the essence as it exists in the mind, where it receives properties like being a subject or predicate, particular or universal, essential or accidental. The author emphasizes that in external reality, there are no such logical categories as subject, predicate, premise, or syllogism; these are mental attributes. All these distinctions are known through reflection (ta'ammul). As an Islamic example, consider the essence 'human': in itself it is just humanity; externally it exists as Zayd or 'Amr; mentally it is a universal subject of propositions like 'Human is rational.'

Key Terms
ماهية (mahiyya) - essence
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni) - mental existence
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji) - external existence
حمل (haml) - predication

Key Takeaway
Essence has three considerations: itself, externally, and mentally; logical attributes like predication are mental accidents and do not exist in external reality.
