The Infinite Regress Objection and Its Response
Author responds to the infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection that every assent requires infinite prior conceptions, and understand the author's response that relative matters need not be finite.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in assent is not a problem because relative matters are inherently non-finite.

A keen objector raises a profound challenge: if every judgment requires conceiving the relation between subject and predicate, and that relation itself has an essence that must be conceived, then an infinite chain of conceptions would be needed. The author's response is surprising and points to a deeper ontology of relations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: assent judges a relation; that relation must be conceived, and it is distinct from the terms. But then the relation has an essence and an occurrence, each requiring conception, leading to infinite regress.
This is similar to the third man argument.
2
Find the author's response
The author does not deny the regress; he says: suppose it is so, but relative matters (al-umur al-nisbiyya) are not required to be finite; they are non-finite by nature.
The response is pragmatic and ontological.
3
Reflect on the implication
The response suggests that the infinite regress is not vicious because relations are not independent entities that need exhaustive conceptualization.
This anticipates later discussions on the nature of relations.
4
Connect to later discussions
The author says this is known later, hinting at a broader doctrine about the nature of relations.
The full justification is beyond this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يلزم منه أن يكون أحد قسميه تصورًا. فكما أن العلم ينقسم إلى التصور وإلى غيره وهو التصديق، فكذلك التصوُّر ينقسم إلى العلم وإلى غيره وهو الجهل. وعلى هذا في التصديق؛ إذ التصديق لا يكون قسما من قسمي العلم؛ بل قسم من قسمي العلم هو التصديق، والتصديق ينقسم إلى العلم وإلى غيره (۱). وقول من قال في مثل هذه الصورة: «إنه خاص من وجه وعام من وجه فذلك باطل. فإنه إذا كان كذلك فلا يكون بينهما خصوصية ولا عمومية، بل الخصوصية والعمومية بين هذا وبين البعض من ذلك؛ وبالعكس. ثم القسمة لا تكون منحصرة في قسمي التصور والتصديق عقلا بل تكون منحصرة من حيث إن التصور عبارة عن إدراك ماهية الشيء من غير أن يحكم عليها أصلا لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات كما مرَّ في التصديق هو الإدراك مع الحكم عليها؛ والشيء بعد الإدراك لا يخلو من أن يحكم عليه أو لا يحكم عليه أصلا. والحكم على الشيء قد يكون بوجود ذلك الشيء، وقد يكون بعدمه. وكما أنه قد يكون بوجود ذلك الشيء وبعدمه كذلك قد يكون بوجود شيء آخر له، وقد يكون بعدم شيء آخر، له، كذلك كقولنا زيد عالم أو ليس بعالم (٢). ولا مجال للحكم بالشيء على الشيء إلا وأن يتصور المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والنسبة وتصور النسبة غير تصور المحكوم عليه وغير تصور المحكوم به كذلك؛ وذلك لأنه يمكن تصور معنى العالم ومعنى الحادث مع الذهول (۳) عن النسبة بينهما فالعالم من حيث هو العالم لا يكون من الأمور النسبية. وكذلك الحادث من حيث هو الحادث. فأما إذا قلنا: العالم حادث فإنهما من الأمور النسبية حينئذ. ثم لقائل أن يقول: لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التصديق عبارة عن الحكم بحصول تلك النسبة. وكما أنه مسبوق بتصور ماهية النسبة فكذلك يتصور الغير من المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون كل تصديق مسبوقاً بتصورات لا نهاية لها؛ وذلك لأن النسبة الحاصلة مغايرة لذات المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به ولتلك النسبة ماهية وحصول وماهية مسبوقة، أي ماهية النسبة الحاصلة (۱) بالحصول لا محالة فيحصل هناك أمور ثلاثة أخرى. وعلى هذا إلى غير النهاية، لكنا نقول: هب، أنه كذلك وإنه كل تصديق مسبوق بتصورات لا نهاية لها، لكن الأمور النسبية لا يلزمها أن تكون متناهية بل لا تكون متناهية؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
It follows that one of its two divisions is conception. For just as knowledge is divided into conception and into something else which is assent, so conception is divided into knowledge and into something else which is ignorance. And likewise for assent; for assent is not a division of the two divisions of knowledge; rather a division of the two divisions of knowledge is assent, and assent is divided into knowledge and into something else (1). And the statement of one who says about such a form: 'It is specific in one respect and general in another' is false. For if it were so, there would be neither specificity nor generality between them; rather specificity and generality exist between this and some of that, and vice versa. Then the division is not restricted to the two divisions of conception and assent by reason, but is restricted in that conception is the apprehension of the essence of a thing without judging it at all, neither by negation nor affirmation, as was said, while assent is apprehension with judgment upon it; and a thing after apprehension is either judged or not judged at all. And judgment upon a thing may be by the existence of that thing, or by its non-existence. And just as it may be by the existence of that thing or its non-existence, likewise it may be by the existence of something else for it, or by the non-existence of something else for it, such as our statement: Zayd is knowledgeable or not knowledgeable (2). And there is no room for judging a thing by a thing unless the subject, the predicate, and the relation are conceived, and the conception of the relation is other than the conception of the subject and other than the conception of the predicate; because it is possible to conceive the meaning of knowledgeable and the meaning of originated while being heedless (3) of the relation between them; for 'knowledgeable' insofar as it is 'knowledgeable' is not among relative matters, and likewise 'originated' insofar as it is 'originated'. But when we say: 'The world is originated', then they become relative matters at that time. Then an objector may say: If it were as mentioned, then assent would be the judgment of the occurrence of that relation. And just as it is preceded by the conception of the essence of the relation, so it requires the conception of the other of the subject and predicate, and then it follows that every assent is preceded by infinite conceptions; because the occurring relation is distinct from the essence of the subject and predicate, and that relation has an essence, an occurrence, and a preceded essence, i.e., the essence of the occurring relation (1) by occurrence necessarily, so three other things occur there. And so on ad infinitum. But we say: Suppose it is so, and every assent is preceded by infinite conceptions; however, relative matters are not required to be finite, rather they are not finite; and that is known later.

Infinite Regress in Tasdiq: Objection and Reply
Author defends his theory of tasdiq against the charge of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's infinite regress argument against the definition of tasdiq and explain the author's reply using the concept of relative matters.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is not a defect for definitions involving relative matters.

In this passage, the author defends his definition of tasdiq (assent) as a judgment about a relation. An objector raises a classic infinite regress problem: if understanding tasdiq requires conceiving the relation, and the relation itself has a quiddity and existence, then we need infinite conceptions. The author responds by conceding the regress but denying it is a problem. He argues that relative matters (umūr nisbiyya) are inherently infinite, so the regress is harmless. This shows how logical definitions can handle seemingly problematic infinite chains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
Find the objector's claim: that if tasdiq is judgment of a relation, then it requires prior conception of that relation, leading to a regress.
The objector starts with 'ثم لقائل أن يقول'
2
Trace the regress
Notice that the relation itself has a quiddity (māhiyya) and existence (ḥuṣūl), each requiring conception, generating an infinite chain.
The objector lists three things: the relation's quiddity, its existence, and the quiddity of the relation existing.
3
Find the author's concession
The author says 'هب، أنه كذلك' (Granted that it is so) – he accepts that every tasdiq is preceded by infinite conceptions.
This shows he does not deny the regress.
4
Locate the author's defense
The author argues that relative matters are not required to be finite; they are infinite by nature.
Key phrase: 'الأمور النسبية لا يلزمها أن تكون متناهية بل لا تكون متناهية'
5
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
The author's defense shifts the burden: the objector assumed finitude is necessary, but the author shows it is not for relational entities.
Thus the objection does not refute the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول: لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التصديق عبارة عن الحكم بحصول تلك النسبة. وكما أنه مسبوق بتصور ماهية النسبة فكذلك يتصور الغير من المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون كل تصديق مسبوقاً بتصورات لا نهاية لها؛ وذلك لأن النسبة الحاصلة مغايرة لذات المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به ولتلك النسبة ماهية وحصول وماهية مسبوقة، أي ماهية النسبة الحاصلة بالحصول لا محالة فيحصل هناك أمور ثلاثة أخرى. وعلى هذا إلى غير النهاية، لكنا نقول: هب، أنه كذلك وإنه كل تصديق مسبوق بتصورات لا نهاية لها، لكن الأمور النسبية لا يلزمها أن تكون متناهية بل لا تكون متناهية؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
Then a speaker may say: If it were as mentioned, then tasdiq would be a judgment of the occurrence of that relation. And just as it is preceded by a conception of the quiddity of the relation, so too the other [terms] of the subject and predicate are conceived. Then it would follow that every tasdiq is preceded by infinite conceptions; because the relation that occurs is other than the essence of the subject and predicate, and that relation has a quiddity and an existence and a quiddity preceded [by existence], i.e., the quiddity of the relation occurring necessarily by existence, so three other things occur. And so on ad infinitum. But we say: Granted that it is so and that every tasdiq is preceded by infinite conceptions, yet relative matters are not required to be finite; rather they are not finite. And that is known later.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. The objector argues against the author's earlier definition of tasdiq as a judgment about a relation. The objector claims that if tasdiq requires conception of the relation, and the relation itself has a quiddity and existence, then this leads to an infinite regress of conceptions required for a single tasdiq. The author responds by conceding the regress but denying that it is a problem. He argues that relative matters (umūr nisbiyya) are not required to be finite; they can be infinite without issue. He says this is known later (presumably from the discussion of infinite regress in logic or metaphysics). The author's point is that the infinite regress of conceptions does not invalidate his definition of tasdiq because relative matters are infinite by nature.

Distinguishing Conception from Affirmation
Author defines types of knowledge and ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple conception and conception with affirmation using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare conception or conception with judgment; the latter is the basis of propositions.

The author begins by separating two primary modes of knowing: mere conception of a term (like understanding the word 'triangle') and conception accompanied by a judgment (like affirming a property of a triangle). This distinction is the bedrock of Islamic logic and epistemology. We will unpack these two types and see how they apply to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Read the opening of the passage: 'a thing may be known by a simple conception' and 'may be known by a conception accompanied by affirmation'. Note the two categories: tasawwur sādhaj (simple conception) and tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq (conception with affirmation).
2
Define simple conception
The passage defines simple conception as 'conception devoid of affirmation'. This means you understand the meaning of a word without judging anything about its existence or truth. For example, knowing the meaning of the word 'triangle' is a simple conception.
3
Define conception with affirmation
This is a conception that includes a judgment (tasdiq). The author's example: 'our knowledge that every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles'. Here, the concept of triangle is accompanied by an affirmative statement about it.
4
Analyze the ignorance counterpart
The passage also describes ignorance in relation to conception (not knowing the meaning of a term) and ignorance in relation to affirmation (not knowing a fact). This reinforces the division: we can be ignorant of a concept or ignorant of a judgment.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the term 'zakāt'. A simple conception is knowing that zakāt means 'purification' or a specific obligatory charity. A conception with affirmation is knowing that 'zakāt is obligatory upon every eligible Muslim'. The latter includes a judgment (obligation).
This shows how the tool works in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشيء قد يعلم تصورًا ساذجا مثل علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث، فهو شكل يحيط به ثلاثة أضلع؛ إذ هو تصور ساذج ؛ والتصور الساذج هو التصور العاري عن التصديق. ومثل هذا التصور لا يكون تصور الشيء بحسب الحقيقة، مثل علمنا بمعنى المثلث؛ إذ ذلك لا يتعرى عن التصديق وهو كونه موجودا في الذهن بخلاف علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث. وقد يعلم تصورا معه تصديق مثل علمنا إن كل مثلث فإن زواياه مساوية لقائمتين. فكذلك وقد يجهل من جهة التصوُّر فلا يتصور معناه إلى أن يتعرف مثل ذي الاسمين والمنفصل وقد يجهل من جهة التصديق إلى أن يتعلم مثل كون القُطر قويا على ضلعي القائمة التي يوترها. ثم الجهل لا ينقسم من حيث هو الجهل، بل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام في العلم لما أنه ينقسم انقسام المعلومات؛ وإنها متميزة بخلاف المجهولات. فالعلم إذن ينقسم إلى التصور والتصديق ابتداء؛ والجهل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام إليهما بناء، واكتساب المجهول بالمعلوم لا محالة. وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفة حال المعلومات وانتظامها في أنفسها حتّى تفيد العلم بالمجهول. فإن لكل مجهول معلومات تناسبه فلمجهول التصور معلومات تصورية ولمجهول التصديق معلومات تصديقية. وتلك المعلومات، إما أن تكون بديهية وهي التي تكون حاصلة بالفطرة من غير أن يتقدمها من المعلومات ما يكون سببًا لحصولها مثل العلم بالوجود والعدم والجزء والكل، والعلم بأن الشيء إما يكون وإما أن لا يكون، وأن كل شيء أعظم من جزئه ونحو ذلك، وإما أن تكون كسبيةً وهي التي تكون حاصلة بمعلومات سابقة عليها مترتبة ترتيبا مخصوصا يتأدى إلى العلم بالمطلوب المجهول لكن لا على سبيل التسلسل، بل من اللوازم أن ينتهي إلى معلومات حاصلة بالفطرة كما مر . فتكون المعلومات إما بديهية من التصورات والتصديقات، وإما كسبية مثل العلم بالهيولى والصورة والعقل والنفس والعلم بأن العالم ممكن والممكن حادث مثلا؛ وإنه من جملة البديهيات فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون العلوم بأجمعها بديهية؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا لا نعلم من الأشياء كلا ولا جملة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بأجمعها كذلك ؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا نعلم من الأشياء
Then a thing may be known by a simple conception, like our knowledge of the meaning of the word 'triangle': it is a shape surrounded by three sides; for it is a simple conception. And simple conception is conception devoid of affirmation. And such a conception is not a conception of the thing according to reality, like our knowledge of the meaning of 'triangle'; for that does not strip away affirmation, i.e., its existence in the mind, unlike our knowledge of the meaning of the word 'triangle'. And it may be known by a conception accompanied by affirmation, like our knowledge that every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles. Similarly, one may be ignorant with respect to conception and thus not conceive its meaning until he learns it, like [the meaning of] 'the thing with two names' and 'the discrete'; and one may be ignorant with respect to affirmation until he learns, like the fact that the diameter is longer than the sides of the right angle that it subtends. Then ignorance is not divided as ignorance per se, but it is divided according to the division of knowledge, since knowledge is divided according to the division of the known things; and they are distinct, unlike the unknown. Knowledge, therefore, is divided into conception and affirmation primarily; and ignorance is divided according to the division of these two, subordinately. And the acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable. And that is not possible except by knowing the states of known things and their arrangement in themselves so that they yield knowledge of the unknown. For every unknown has known things that correspond to it: for an unknown conception there are conceptual known things, and for an unknown affirmation there are assertoric known things. And those known things are either self-evident, which are obtained by nature without being preceded by any known things that are a cause of their occurrence, like knowledge of existence and non-existence, part and whole, and knowledge that a thing either is or is not, and that everything is greater than its part, and so on; or they are acquired, which are obtained by prior known things arranged in a specific order that leads to knowledge of the sought unknown, but not in an infinite regress; rather one of the necessities is that it ends with known things obtained by nature, as before. So the known things are either self-evident in terms of conceptions and affirmations, or acquired, like knowledge of matter and form, intellect and soul, and knowledge that the world is possible and the possible is originated, for example. And it is part of the self-evident [truths] that it is impossible for all sciences to be self-evident; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know things wholly or entirely. And it is impossible for them all to be likewise [acquired]; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we know of things
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies knowledge. The author begins by distinguishing between simple conception (tasawwur sādhaj: conception without judgment) and conception accompanied by judgment (tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq). He gives the example of knowing the meaning of the word 'triangle' as a simple conception, versus knowing the geometric theorem about triangles as a conception with affirmation. He then discusses ignorance, noting that ignorance is divided according to the division of knowledge, not as a category in itself. Knowledge is primarily divided into conception (tasawwur) and affirmation (tasdiq). Acquiring unknown things from known things requires an understanding of how known things are arranged. Then he divides known things into self-evident (badīhiyy) and acquired (kasbī). Self-evident knowledge is obtained by nature without prior cause, such as the principles of existence, non-existence, part/whole, etc. Acquired knowledge requires prior known things arranged in a specific order that leads to the sought unknown, but this chain must end with self-evident truths to avoid infinite regress. Examples of acquired knowledge include knowledge of hyle, form, intellect, soul, and the contingency and origination of the world. He further argues that not all knowledge can be self-evident, nor can it all be acquired, as we know by self-evidence that we do not have exhaustive knowledge. The passage thus lays the foundation for the need for logic as a tool for acquiring unknown knowledge from known knowledge.

Self-evident vs. Acquired Knowledge
Author divides known things into self-evident and acquired

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify pieces of knowledge as either self-evident (badihi) or acquired (kasbi) and explain why acquired knowledge must ultimately rest on self-evident truths.

Core Idea
All acquired knowledge rests on self-evident truths; logic chains end in axioms to avoid infinite regress.

After dividing knowledge into conception and affirmation, the author further categorizes known things (both conceptual and assertoric) into self-evident (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). Self-evident knowledge is obtained by nature without prior cause; acquired knowledge requires prior known things arranged in a specific order. This classification ensures that the process of learning does not go on forever but terminates in axioms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of known things
In the passage, after discussing conception and affirmation, the author says: 'those known things are either self-evident... or they are acquired'. Locate this division.
2
Define self-evident knowledge (badihi)
Self-evident knowledge is 'obtained by nature from the outset' without being preceded by any known things that cause it. Examples: knowledge of existence, non-existence, part and whole, and that a thing either is or is not.
3
Define acquired knowledge (kasbi)
Acquired knowledge is 'obtained by prior known things arranged in a specific order that leads to knowledge of the sought unknown'. The author emphasizes that this chain does not go on infinitely but must end in self-evident truths.
4
Recognize examples from the passage
Self-evident examples: 'existence and non-existence', 'everything is greater than its part'. Acquired examples: 'knowledge of matter and form, intellect and soul', and 'that the world is possible and the possible is originated'.
5
Explain the impossibility of infinite regress
The passage states that acquired knowledge cannot form an infinite chain (tasalsul). It must end in self-evident truths. This is a key principle: every proof ultimately relies on foundational axioms.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشيء قد يعلم تصورًا ساذجا مثل علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث، فهو شكل يحيط به ثلاثة أضلع؛ إذ هو تصور ساذج ؛ والتصور الساذج هو التصور العاري عن التصديق. ومثل هذا التصور لا يكون تصور الشيء بحسب الحقيقة، مثل علمنا بمعنى المثلث؛ إذ ذلك لا يتعرى عن التصديق وهو كونه موجودا في الذهن بخلاف علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث. وقد يعلم تصورا معه تصديق مثل علمنا إن كل مثلث فإن زواياه مساوية لقائمتين. فكذلك وقد يجهل من جهة التصوُّر فلا يتصور معناه إلى أن يتعرف مثل ذي الاسمين والمنفصل وقد يجهل من جهة التصديق إلى أن يتعلم مثل كون القُطر قويا على ضلعي القائمة التي يوترها. ثم الجهل لا ينقسم من حيث هو الجهل، بل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام في العلم لما أنه ينقسم انقسام المعلومات؛ وإنها متميزة بخلاف المجهولات. فالعلم إذن ينقسم إلى التصور والتصديق ابتداء؛ والجهل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام إليهما بناء، واكتساب المجهول بالمعلوم لا محالة. وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفة حال المعلومات وانتظامها في أنفسها حتّى تفيد العلم بالمجهول. فإن لكل مجهول معلومات تناسبه فلمجهول التصور معلومات تصورية ولمجهول التصديق معلومات تصديقية. وتلك المعلومات، إما أن تكون بديهية وهي التي تكون حاصلة بالفطرة من غير أن يتقدمها من المعلومات ما يكون سببًا لحصولها مثل العلم بالوجود والعدم والجزء والكل، والعلم بأن الشيء إما يكون وإما أن لا يكون، وأن كل شيء أعظم من جزئه ونحو ذلك، وإما أن تكون كسبيةً وهي التي تكون حاصلة بمعلومات سابقة عليها مترتبة ترتيبا مخصوصا يتأدى إلى العلم بالمطلوب المجهول لكن لا على سبيل التسلسل، بل من اللوازم أن ينتهي إلى معلومات حاصلة بالفطرة كما مر . فتكون المعلومات إما بديهية من التصورات والتصديقات، وإما كسبية مثل العلم بالهيولى والصورة والعقل والنفس والعلم بأن العالم ممكن والممكن حادث مثلا؛ وإنه من جملة البديهيات فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون العلوم بأجمعها بديهية؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا لا نعلم من الأشياء كلا ولا جملة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بأجمعها كذلك ؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا نعلم من الأشياء
Then a thing may be known by a simple conception, like our knowledge of the meaning of the word 'triangle': it is a shape surrounded by three sides; for it is a simple conception. And simple conception is conception devoid of affirmation. And such a conception is not a conception of the thing according to reality, like our knowledge of the meaning of 'triangle'; for that does not strip away affirmation, i.e., its existence in the mind, unlike our knowledge of the meaning of the word 'triangle'. And it may be known by a conception accompanied by affirmation, like our knowledge that every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles. Similarly, one may be ignorant with respect to conception and thus not conceive its meaning until he learns it, like [the meaning of] 'the thing with two names' and 'the discrete'; and one may be ignorant with respect to affirmation until he learns, like the fact that the diameter is longer than the sides of the right angle that it subtends. Then ignorance is not divided as ignorance per se, but it is divided according to the division of knowledge, since knowledge is divided according to the division of the known things; and they are distinct, unlike the unknown. Knowledge, therefore, is divided into conception and affirmation primarily; and ignorance is divided according to the division of these two, subordinately. And the acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable. And that is not possible except by knowing the states of known things and their arrangement in themselves so that they yield knowledge of the unknown. For every unknown has known things that correspond to it: for an unknown conception there are conceptual known things, and for an unknown affirmation there are assertoric known things. And those known things are either self-evident, which are obtained by nature without being preceded by any known things that are a cause of their occurrence, like knowledge of existence and non-existence, part and whole, and knowledge that a thing either is or is not, and that everything is greater than its part, and so on; or they are acquired, which are obtained by prior known things arranged in a specific order that leads to knowledge of the sought unknown, but not in an infinite regress; rather one of the necessities is that it ends with known things obtained by nature, as before. So the known things are either self-evident in terms of conceptions and affirmations, or acquired, like knowledge of matter and form, intellect and soul, and knowledge that the world is possible and the possible is originated, for example. And it is part of the self-evident [truths] that it is impossible for all sciences to be self-evident; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know things wholly or entirely. And it is impossible for them all to be likewise [acquired]; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we know of things

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies knowledge. The author begins by distinguishing between simple conception (tasawwur sādhaj: conception without judgment) and conception accompanied by judgment (tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq). He gives the example of knowing the meaning of the word 'triangle' as a simple conception, versus knowing the geometric theorem about triangles as a conception with affirmation. He then discusses ignorance, noting that ignorance is divided according to the division of knowledge, not as a category in itself. Examples of acquired knowledge include knowledge of hyle, form, intellect, soul, and the contingency and origination of the world. He further argues that not all knowledge can be self-evident, nor can it all be acquired, as we know by self-evidence that we do not have exhaustive knowledge. Self-Evident vs Acquired Knowledge
Author divides knowledge into conception and assent, and into self-evident and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given piece of knowledge is self-evident (badihi) or acquired (kasbi) and explain the difference using examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Knowledge rests on a foundation of self-evident truths that require no proof.

This passage establishes the foundational division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), and further into self-evident (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). Understanding this division is critical for any student of Islamic logic, because it determines what counts as a starting point in reasoning. The author gives concrete examples from metaphysics and theology to illustrate self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary divisions of knowledge
The author first divides knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq, then each into badihi and kasbi. Find these terms in the Arabic passage and note how they relate.
The division of ignorance follows that of knowledge.
2
Distinguish badihi from kasbi based on definitions
Badihi is obtained by fitra without any prior knowledge causing it; kasbi requires prior known things arranged in a specific order.
The definition of badihi includes the phrase 'min ghayri an yataqaddamaha min al-malumat'.
3
Find examples of each type
Badihi examples: existence, non-existence, part-whole. Kasbi examples: knowledge of hyle, form, intellect, soul, and that the world is possible.
Note that the author includes some theological propositions as self-evident.
4
Explain why this division is necessary for logic
The logician must study how known things compose to lead to the unknown; this division justifies starting points.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجهل لا ينقسم من حيث هو الجهل، بل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام في العلم لما أنه ينقسم انقسام المعلومات؛ وإنها متميزة بخلاف المجهولات. فالعلم إذن ينقسم إلى التصور والتصديق ابتداء؛ والجهل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام إليهما بناء، واكتساب المجهول بالمعلوم لا محالة. وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفة حال المعلومات وانتظامها في أنفسها حتّى تفيد العلم بالمجهول. فإن لكل مجهول معلومات تناسبه فلمجهول التصور معلومات تصورية ولمجهول التصديق معلومات تصديقية. وتلك المعلومات، إما أن تكون بديهية وهي التي تكون حاصلة بالفطرة من غير أن يتقدمها من المعلومات ما يكون سببًا لحصولها مثل العلم بالوجود والعدم والجزء والكل، والعلم بأن الشيء إما يكون وإما أن لا يكون، وأن كل شيء أعظم من جزئه ونحو ذلك، وإما أن تكون كسبيةً وهي التي تكون حاصلة بمعلومات سابقة عليها مترتبة ترتيبا مخصوصا يتأدى إلى العلم بالمطلوب المجهول لكن لا على سبيل التسلسل، بل من اللوازم أن ينتهي إلى معلومات حاصلة بالفطرة كما مر . فتكون المعلومات إما بديهية من التصورات والتصديقات، وإما كسبية مثل العلم بالهيولى والصورة والعقل والنفس والعلم بأن العالم ممكن والممكن حادث مثلا؛ وإنه من جملة البديهيات فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون العلوم بأجمعها بديهية؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا لا نعلم من الأشياء كلا ولا جملة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بأجمعها كذلك ؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا نعلم من الأشياء ما تعلمه. وقد قيل فيه: «إنه لا يمكن البتة، وإلا يلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل فإنه إذا افتقر الكل إلى الكسب»؛ وذلك هو الفكر، والفكر هنا الفكر المتعتب للعزم هو الانتقال من الأمور الحاضرة في الذهن إلى أمور غير حاضرة، كانت تلك الأمور كسبية لا محالة. فالكاسبة لها إن كان هي هذه الأمور فقد لزم الدور ، وإن كانت غيرها فقد لزم التسلسل ؛ ولا سبيل إلى كل واحد منهما على ما عرف. ولما كانت العلوم منقسمة إلى أن تكون بديهية وإلى أن تكون كسبية ولا يلزم أن تكون العلوم السابقة بديهية أو كسبية بل يلزم أن تكون مرتبة ترتيبا خاصا؛ فيلزم للمنطقي إذن أن ينظر في تلك العلوم السابقة وكيفية تأليفها وتأديتها إلى المجهولات المطلوبة.
Then ignorance is not divided in terms of being ignorance, but is divided according to the division in knowledge because it divides according to the division of known things; and they are distinct unlike unknown things. Knowledge therefore divides into tasawwur and tasdiq initially; and ignorance divides according to the division into them accordingly. Acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable. That is only possible by knowing the state of the known things and their ordering in themselves so that they yield knowledge of the unknown. For each unknown there are suitable known things: for the unknown of tasawwur, there are tasawwur-related known things, and for the unknown of tasdiq, there are tasdiq-related known things. Those known things are either self-evident (badihi), which are obtained by innate disposition without any prior known things being the cause of their acquisition, like knowledge of existence and non-existence, part and whole, and knowledge that a thing either exists or does not exist, and that every whole is greater than its part, and similar; and they are either acquired (kasbi), which are obtained by prior known things arranged in a specific order leading to knowledge of the unknown desired, but not in an infinite regress way; rather it is necessary that they end with innate known things as mentioned. So the known things are either self-evident from tasawwur and tasdiq, or acquired, like knowledge of hylè and form, intellect and soul, and knowledge that the world is possible and the possible is originated, for example; and that is among the self-evident things, for it is impossible that all sciences are self-evident; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know all things nor the whole. And it is impossible that all are like that; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we know some of the things we know. And it has been said concerning this: 'It is absolutely impossible, otherwise either circularity or infinite regress would follow, for if all depends on acquisition' - that is reflection (fikr), and reflection here is the reflection that is considered for determination: it is the transition from matters present in the mind to matters not present; those matters are necessarily acquired. So the acqurier of them if it is these matters, then circularity follows, and if it is other than them, infinite regress follows; and there is no way to either of them as has been established. Since the sciences are divided into being self-evident and being acquired, and it is not necessary that the prior sciences are self-evident or acquired, but rather they must be arranged in a specific order, it is therefore necessary for the logician to examine those prior sciences and how they are composed and how they lead to the unknown desired.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing the division of ignorance (jahl), noting that it is not divided qua ignorance but according to the division of knowledge. Since knowledge is initially divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), ignorance is divided accordingly. The author then states that acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable, and this requires understanding the arrangement of known things. The known things are two kinds: self-evident (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). Self-evident are those obtained by innate disposition without prior cause, like knowledge of existence, non-existence, part and whole, etc. Acquired are those obtained from prior known things arranged in a specific order leading to the unknown, but the chain must end with self-evident truths to avoid infinite regress. The author emphasizes that not all sciences can be self-evident; we know by self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know all things. The argument against all knowledge being acquired is presented: if all depended on acquisition, then either circularity (dal) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, both impossible. Reflection (fikr) is defined as the transition from present to absent matters. Since sciences are divided into self-evident and acquired, the logician must study the prior sciences and their composition to lead to the unknown.

Refutation of Infinite Regress and Circularity
Author refutes the possibility that all knowledge is acquired by showing it leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that not all knowledge can be acquired and explain why circularity and infinite regress are impossible.

Core Idea
If every piece of knowledge required prior knowledge, we would never know anything.

The author presents a powerful argument: if all knowledge were acquired (kasbi), then either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would result, both of which are impossible. This establishes the necessity of self-evident truths as the foundation. Understanding this refutation is essential for any student of Islamic logic, as it governs how reasoning chains are constructed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that all knowledge is acquired (kasbi). The author argues this cannot be true.
2
Extract the conditional argument
If all knowledge were acquired, then it would depend on prior knowledge. That chain would either loop back (circularity) or extend infinitely (infinite regress). Find this conditional in the Arabic: 'if all depends on acquisition, then either circularity or infinite regress.'
3
Explain why both options are impossible
Circularity is impossible because it would mean knowledge depends on itself. Infinite regress is impossible because there would be no starting point. Both are rejected by the intellect.
The author says 'there is no way to either of them'.
4
State the conclusion
Therefore, some knowledge must be self-evident (badihi). The chain of acquired knowledge ends with self-evident truths.

ثم الجهل لا ينقسم من حيث هو الجهل، بل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام في العلم لما أنه ينقسم انقسام المعلومات؛ وإنها متميزة بخلاف المجهولات. فالعلم إذن ينقسم إلى التصور والتصديق ابتداء؛ والجهل ينقسم بحسب الانقسام إليهما بناء، واكتساب المجهول بالمعلوم لا محالة. وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفة حال المعلومات وانتظامها في أنفسها حتّى تفيد العلم بالمجهول. فإن لكل مجهول معلومات تناسبه فلمجهول التصور معلومات تصورية ولمجهول التصديق معلومات تصديقية. وتلك المعلومات، إما أن تكون بديهية وهي التي تكون حاصلة بالفطرة من غير أن يتقدمها من المعلومات ما يكون سببًا لحصولها مثل العلم بالوجود والعدم والجزء والكل، والعلم بأن الشيء إما يكون وإما أن لا يكون، وأن كل شيء أعظم من جزئه ونحو ذلك، وإما أن تكون كسبيةً وهي التي تكون حاصلة بمعلومات سابقة عليها مترتبة ترتيبا مخصوصا يتأدى إلى العلم بالمطلوب المجهول لكن لا على سبيل التسلسل، بل من اللوازم أن ينتهي إلى معلومات حاصلة بالفطرة كما مر . فتكون المعلومات إما بديهية من التصورات والتصديقات، وإما كسبية مثل العلم بالهيولى والصورة والعقل والنفس والعلم بأن العالم ممكن والممكن حادث مثلا؛ وإنه من جملة البديهيات فإنه لا يمكن أن تكون العلوم بأجمعها بديهية؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا لا نعلم من الأشياء كلا ولا جملة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بأجمعها كذلك ؛ وقد علمنا ببديهية العقل أنا نعلم من الأشياء ما تعلمه. وقد قيل فيه: «إنه لا يمكن البتة، وإلا يلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل فإنه إذا افتقر الكل إلى الكسب»؛ وذلك هو الفكر، والفكر هنا الفكر المتعتب للعزم هو الانتقال من الأمور الحاضرة في الذهن إلى أمور غير حاضرة، كانت تلك الأمور كسبية لا محالة. فالكاسبة لها إن كان هي هذه الأمور فقد لزم الدور ، وإن كانت غيرها فقد لزم التسلسل ؛ ولا سبيل إلى كل واحد منهما على ما عرف. ولما كانت العلوم منقسمة إلى أن تكون بديهية وإلى أن تكون كسبية ولا يلزم أن تكون العلوم السابقة بديهية أو كسبية بل يلزم أن تكون مرتبة ترتيبا خاصا؛ فيلزم للمنطقي إذن أن ينظر في تلك العلوم السابقة وكيفية تأليفها وتأديتها إلى المجهولات المطلوبة.
Then ignorance is not divided in terms of being ignorance, but is divided according to the division in knowledge because it divides according to the division of known things; and they are distinct unlike unknown things. Knowledge therefore divides into tasawwur and tasdiq initially; and ignorance divides according to the division into them accordingly. Acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable. That is only possible by knowing the state of the known things and their ordering in themselves so that they yield knowledge of the unknown. For each unknown there are suitable known things: for the unknown of tasawwur, there are tasawwur-related known things, and for the unknown of tasdiq, there are tasdiq-related known things. Those known things are either self-evident (badihi), which are obtained by innate disposition without any prior known things being the cause of their acquisition, like knowledge of existence and non-existence, part and whole, and knowledge that a thing either exists or does not exist, and that every whole is greater than its part, and similar; and they are either acquired (kasbi), which are obtained by prior known things arranged in a specific order leading to knowledge of the unknown desired, but not in an infinite regress way; rather it is necessary that they end with innate known things as mentioned. So the known things are either self-evident from tasawwur and tasdiq, or acquired, like knowledge of hylè and form, intellect and soul, and knowledge that the world is possible and the possible is originated, for example; and that is among the self-evident things, for it is impossible that all sciences are self-evident; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know all things nor the whole. And it is impossible that all are like that; and we know by the self-evidence of the intellect that we know some of the things we know. And it has been said concerning this: 'It is absolutely impossible, otherwise either circularity or infinite regress would follow, for if all depends on acquisition' - that is reflection (fikr), and reflection here is the reflection that is considered for determination: it is the transition from matters present in the mind to matters not present; those matters are necessarily acquired. So the acqurier of them if it is these matters, then circularity follows, and if it is other than them, infinite regress follows; and there is no way to either of them as has been established. Since the sciences are divided into being self-evident and being acquired, and it is not necessary that the prior sciences are self-evident or acquired, but rather they must be arranged in a specific order, it is therefore necessary for the logician to examine those prior sciences and how they are composed and how they lead to the unknown desired.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing the division of ignorance (jahl), noting that it is not divided qua ignorance but according to the division of knowledge. Since knowledge is initially divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), ignorance is divided accordingly. The author then states that acquisition of the unknown from the known is inevitable, and this requires understanding the arrangement of known things. The known things are two kinds: self-evident (badihi) and acquired (kasbi). Self-evident are those obtained by innate disposition without prior cause, like knowledge of existence, non-existence, part and whole, etc. Acquired are those obtained from prior known things arranged in a specific order leading to the unknown, but the chain must end with self-evident truths to avoid infinite regress. The author emphasizes that not all sciences can be self-evident; we know by self-evidence of the intellect that we do not know all things. The argument against all knowledge being acquired is presented: if all depended on acquisition, then either circularity (dal) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, both impossible. Reflection (fikr) is defined as the transition from present to absent matters. Since sciences are divided into self-evident and acquired, the logician must study the prior sciences and their composition to lead to the unknown.

تسلسل
فكر

A) Because we don't know everything
B) Because it would require either circularity or infinite regress
C) Because self-evident things are more certain

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of circularity and infinite regress proves that self-evident knowledge is necessary as the foundation for all acquired knowledge.

The Three Types of Indication: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام
Author explains the three types of linguistic indication and the distinction between literal and metaphorical usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three types of linguistic indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and their logical relationships.

Core Idea
Words indicate meanings in three ways: by correspondence, by inclusion, and by implication.

This lesson introduces the foundational conceptual taxonomy of indication (dalala) in Islamic logic. The author distinguishes three types: correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). Understanding these types is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types from the passage
Find the Arabic terms مطابقة, تضمن, التزام in the passage. Note that they are the three divisions of indication (دلالة).
2
Understand the conventional vs. rational distinction
The author states that mutabaqa is conventional (وضعية), while tadammun and iltizam are rational (عقلية). This means the latter require inference beyond the word's coinage.
3
Analyze the dependency relationships
Tadammun cannot exist without mutabaqa, but mutabaqa can exist without tadammun (e.g., 'substance' indicates its meaning without implying part). Mutabaqa and iltizam are mutually necessary: neither exists without the other.
This is a key logical nuance: every word that indicates by correspondence also implies a necessary concomitant, and vice versa.
4
Address the metonymy objection
The author raises a potential objection: why not five types due to metonymy (كناية)? He argues that metonymy ultimately falls under correspondence if the literal meaning is intended, so the three-fold division stands.
5
Apply the taxonomy to a new example
Take the word 'خمر' (wine). Its literal meaning is the fermented drink (mutabaqa), its intoxicating property is a necessary concomitant (iltizam), and its liquid state is a part of its meaning (tadammun).
This example shows how the three types overlap in practice.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدلالة في الأول من هذه الثلاثة هي الوضعية على خلاف الدلالة في الثاني منها والثالث ؛ إذ هي من الدلالات العقلية فيهما ولا وجود للثانية منها بدون الأولى البتة بخلاف الأولى. فإنها قد توجد بدون الثانية كدلالة لفظ الجوهر على معناه والعرض كذلك، لكنها - وهي الأولى منها - لا توجد البتة بدون الثالثة ولا الثالثة بدونها كذلك. ولا يقال كيف هي والقسمة غير منحصرة في هذه الثلاثة لما أن اللفظ كما أنه يدل على المسمى وعلى لازمه داخلا فيه وخارجًا عنه. فذلك يدل عليه وعلى ملزومه كذلك بطريق الكناية داخلا فيه وخارجًا عنه؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون القسمة منحصرة في الخمسة؛ إذ اللفظ في الكناية يطلق على المعنى بطريق المطابقة، كما في قولك طويل النجاد مثلا، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يريد طول نجاده من غير أن كان تأويل، وإن كان المراد طول القامة. ولو كان كذلك لكانت القسمة منحصرة في الثلاثة المذكورة لا غير واللفظ يطلق في الأول منها بطريق الحقيقة وفي الثاني والثالث بطريق المجاز في الثاني إطلاق لفظ الكل على الجزء من حيث هو جزؤه، وهذا القيد من اللوازم؛ فإن من الألفاظ ما يدل على الكل بطريق المطابقة وعلى الجزء كذلك، كلفظ الإمكان مثلا، فإنه يدل على الإمكان الخاص بطريق المطابقة، وعلى جزئه -وهو الإمكان العام - كذلك. وفي الثالث إطلاق لفظ الملزوم على اللازم من حيث هو لازمه، وهذا القيد أيضًا من اللوازم؛ فإن من الألفاظ ما يدل على الملزوم بطريق المطابقة وعلى لازمه أيضًا، كلفظ الشمس، فإنه يدل على قرص الشمس بطريق المطابقة وعلى لازمه وهو الضوء الواقع على الأحجار والأشجار كذلك. واللفظ في هذا القسم هو الذي يدل بطريق المطابقة وهو الحقيقة؛ إذ الحقيقة والمجاز هما وصفا اللفظ، وإنه لا يوصف بهذا أو بذلك إلا بقصد المتكلم وإرادته. فإنه إذا استعمل لتعريف ما وضع بإزائه فهو الحقيقة، وإلا فهو المجاز ؛ إذا كان استعماله على وجه الصحة والحقيقة تعرف بأنها هي اللفظة المستعملة لتعريف ما وضعت هي بإزائه، ولا يعرف بأنها كل لفظه كذا وكذا فإن ذلك باطل، والبطلان فيه ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل. والمجاز يعرف بأنه هو اللفظة المستعملة لتعريف ما وضعت هي بإزاء غيره لملاحظة بينهما، كما هي بين الأسد والشجاع مثلا. فاللفظ في التضمن إذن هو المجاز وفي الالتزام كذلك، واللازم هو الالتزام الذهني لا الخارجي؛ فإن لفظ الجوهر مثلًا لا يستعمل لتعريف العرض وإن كان العرض من لوازمه، ولا لفظ العرض لتعريف الجوهر وإن كان الجوهر من لوازمه أيضًا.
Then, the indication in the first of these three is conventional, contrary to the indication in the second and third of them; for the latter two are rational indications. The second cannot exist without the first at all, unlike the first. For the first may exist without the second, as the indication of the word 'substance' for its meaning, and similarly for 'accident'. However, the first cannot exist without the third, nor the third without it likewise. It cannot be said: 'How is that?' while the division is not restricted to these three, because the word, just as it indicates the named object and its necessary concomitant, sometimes indicates the necessary concomitant as included in it and sometimes as outside it. Thus it indicates it and its implicative concomitant by way of metonymy, whether included or excluded. If that were the case, then the division would be restricted to five. Indeed, the word in metonymy is applied to the meaning by way of correspondence, as in your saying 'long-necked' for example. For there is no impediment to his intending the length of his neck without any interpretation, even if the intended meaning is height. If it were so, the division would be restricted to the aforementioned three only, no other. The word is used in the first by way of reality (haqiqah), and in the second and third by way of metaphor (majaz). In the second, it is the application of the word for the whole to the part insofar as it is a part of it, and this condition is among the entailments. For some words indicate the whole by way of correspondence and also indicate the part likewise, as the word 'possibility' for example: it indicates specific possibility by way of correspondence, and also its part – that is, general possibility – likewise. In the third, it is the application of the word for the implicant (malzum) to the necessary concomitant (lazim) insofar as it is its concomitant, and this condition is also among the entailments. For some words indicate the implicant by way of correspondence and also indicate its necessary concomitant, as the word 'sun' – it indicates the disc of the sun by way of correspondence, and its necessary concomitant – which is the light falling on stones and trees – likewise. The word in this section is that which indicates by way of correspondence, and it is the reality (haqiqah). For reality and metaphor are descriptions of the word, and it is not described by this or that except by the speaker's intent and will. For if it is used to define what it was originally coined for, it is reality; otherwise, it is metaphor – i.e., it is used correctly. And reality is known as: 'the word used to define what it was coined for,' and it is not known as 'every word such and such,' for that is invalid, and its invalidity is apparent upon reflection. Metaphor is known as: 'the word used to define something other than what it was coined for, due to a relation between them,' as between 'lion' and 'brave man' for example. The word in inclusion (tadammun) therefore is metaphor, and likewise in implication (iltizam). The concomitant (lazim) is mental, not external; for the word 'substance,' for example, is not used to define 'accident' even though accident is a necessary concomitant of it, nor is the word 'accident' used to define 'substance' even though substance is a necessary concomitant of it.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the three types of indication (مطابقة, تضمن, التزام) and clarifies the distinction between literal (حقيقة) and metaphorical (مجاز) usage. The author first asserts that the first type, correspondence, is conventional (وضعية), while the other two are rational (عقلية). He explains that the second (inclusion) cannot exist without the first, but the first can exist without the second, as in the word 'substance' indicating its meaning without necessarily implying inclusion. However, the first cannot exist without the third (implication), and vice versa. Then the author addresses a potential objection: why isn't the division expanded to five forms due to metonymy? He argues that metonymy ultimately falls under correspondence if the literal meaning is intended, so the foundational three remain. He then provides examples: 'long-necked' is used literally for length of neck, even if figuratively for height. Next, he defines the literal use as applying a word to what it was coined for, and metaphorical use as applying to something else due to a relation. He gives examples: 'lion' for a brave man. He classifies تضمن and التزام as metaphorical. Finally, he emphasizes that the necessary concomitant (لازم) is mental, not external, because we do not use 'substance' to define 'accident' even though they are related.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalala): Indication; the relationship between a word and its meaning, here divided into three types: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام. [Note: No significant shift across the tradition.]
مطابقة (mutabaqa): Correspondence; the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was coined, used literally. [Note: Stable term across logic texts.]
تضمن (tadammun): Inclusion; the word indicates a part of its meaning, used metaphorically. [Note: Consistently used as one of the three indications.]
التزام (iltizam): Implication; the word indicates something necessarily entailed by its meaning, used metaphorically. [Note: The mental vs external distinction is emphasized here.]
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal usage; a word used for what it was originally coined for. [Note: Stable; but sometimes contrasted with مجاز in terms of frequency.]
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; a word used for something other than its original coinage due to a relation. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the relationship between the three types of indication?
A) Tadammun cannot exist without mutabaqa, but mutabaqa can exist without tadammun.
B) Mutabaqa cannot exist without tadammun, and tadammun cannot exist without mutabaqa.
C) Iltizam and mutabaqa are independent of each other.
D) All three types are equally conventional.

Key Takeaway
The three indications are mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication), with specific logical dependencies.

Literal vs Metaphorical: حقيقة and مجاز
Author explains the three types of linguistic indication and the distinction between literal and metaphorical usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage and classify tadammun and iltizam as metaphorical.

Core Idea
Literal usage is for the original coinage; metaphorical usage is for something else due to a relation.

In this lesson, we learn how the author defines haqiqa and majaz and why tadammun and iltizam are considered metaphorical. The author emphasizes that the necessary concomitant (lazim) is mental, not external, and provides examples like 'sun' for light.

Guided Path
1
Find the definitions of haqiqa and majaz in the passage
The author states: reality (haqiqa) is when a word is used to define what it was coined for; metaphor (majaz) is when it is used for something else due to a relation. Locate these definitions in the Arabic.
2
Note the role of speaker intent
Haqiqa and majaz are not properties of words in isolation; they depend on the speaker's purpose. The author says: 'it is not described by this or that except by the speaker's intent and will.'
3
Examine the examples given
The author gives 'lion' for a brave man as an example of majaz. 'Sun' for its light is an example of mutabaqa (literal) but also indicates its light by iltizam. Understand why the 'sun' example is still literal for the disc.
4
Understand why tadammun and iltizam are majaz
Tadammun applies the whole to a part (e.g., 'possibility' for 'general possibility'), and iltizam applies the implicant to a necessary concomitant (e.g., 'substance' for 'accident' is not done). The passage explicitly says: 'The word in inclusion therefore is metaphor, and likewise in implication.'
5
Clarify that the concomitant is mental, not external
The author stresses that the lazim (necessary concomitant) in iltizam is mental, not external. For example, we do not use 'substance' to define 'accident' even though they are related externally. This refines the understanding of iltizam.

ثم الدلالة في الأول من هذه الثلاثة هي الوضعية على خلاف الدلالة في الثاني منها والثالث ؛ إذ هي من الدلالات العقلية فيهما ولا وجود للثانية منها بدون الأولى البتة بخلاف الأولى. فإنها قد توجد بدون الثانية كدلالة لفظ الجوهر على معناه والعرض كذلك، لكنها - وهي الأولى منها - لا توجد البتة بدون الثالثة ولا الثالثة بدونها كذلك. ولا يقال كيف هي والقسمة غير منحصرة في هذه الثلاثة لما أن اللفظ كما أنه يدل على المسمى وعلى لازمه داخلا فيه وخارجًا عنه. فذلك يدل عليه وعلى ملزومه كذلك بطريق الكناية داخلا فيه وخارجًا عنه؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون القسمة منحصرة في الخمسة؛ إذ اللفظ في الكناية يطلق على المعنى بطريق المطابقة، كما في قولك طويل النجاد مثلا، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يريد طول نجاده من غير أن كان تأويل، وإن كان المراد طول القامة. ولو كان كذلك لكانت القسمة منحصرة في الثلاثة المذكورة لا غير واللفظ يطلق في الأول منها بطريق الحقيقة وفي الثاني والثالث بطريق المجاز في الثاني إطلاق لفظ الكل على الجزء من حيث هو جزؤه، وهذا القيد من اللوازم؛ فإن من الألفاظ ما يدل على الكل بطريق المطابقة وعلى الجزء كذلك، كلفظ الإمكان مثلا، فإنه يدل على الإمكان الخاص بطريق المطابقة، وعلى جزئه -وهو الإمكان العام - كذلك. وفي الثالث إطلاق لفظ الملزوم على اللازم من حيث هو لازمه، وهذا القيد أيضًا من اللوازم؛ فإن من الألفاظ ما يدل على الملزوم بطريق المطابقة وعلى لازمه أيضًا، كلفظ الشمس، فإنه يدل على قرص الشمس بطريق المطابقة وعلى لازمه وهو الضوء الواقع على الأحجار والأشجار كذلك. واللفظ في هذا القسم هو الذي يدل بطريق المطابقة وهو الحقيقة؛ إذ الحقيقة والمجاز هما وصفا اللفظ، وإنه لا يوصف بهذا أو بذلك إلا بقصد المتكلم وإرادته. فإنه إذا استعمل لتعريف ما وضع بإزائه فهو الحقيقة، وإلا فهو المجاز ؛ إذا كان استعماله على وجه الصحة والحقيقة تعرف بأنها هي اللفظة المستعملة لتعريف ما وضعت هي بإزائه، ولا يعرف بأنها كل لفظه كذا وكذا فإن ذلك باطل، والبطلان فيه ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل. والمجاز يعرف بأنه هو اللفظة المستعملة لتعريف ما وضعت هي بإزاء غيره لملاحظة بينهما، كما هي بين الأسد والشجاع مثلا. فاللفظ في التضمن إذن هو المجاز وفي الالتزام كذلك، واللازم هو الالتزام الذهني لا الخارجي؛ فإن لفظ الجوهر مثلًا لا يستعمل لتعريف العرض وإن كان العرض من لوازمه، ولا لفظ العرض لتعريف الجوهر وإن كان الجوهر من لوازمه أيضًا.
Then, the indication in the first of these three is conventional, contrary to the indication in the second and third of them; for the latter two are rational indications. The second cannot exist without the first at all, unlike the first. For the first may exist without the second, as the indication of the word 'substance' for its meaning, and similarly for 'accident'. However, the first cannot exist without the third, nor the third without it likewise. It cannot be said: 'How is that?' while the division is not restricted to these three, because the word, just as it indicates the named object and its necessary concomitant, sometimes indicates the necessary concomitant as included in it and sometimes as outside it. Thus it indicates it and its implicative concomitant by way of metonymy, whether included or excluded. If that were the case, then the division would be restricted to five. Indeed, the word in metonymy is applied to the meaning by way of correspondence, as in your saying 'long-necked' for example. For there is no impediment to his intending the length of his neck without any interpretation, even if the intended meaning is height. If it were so, the division would be restricted to the aforementioned three only, no other. The word is used in the first by way of reality (haqiqah), and in the second and third by way of metaphor (majaz). In the second, it is the application of the word for the whole to the part insofar as it is a part of it, and this condition is among the entailments. For some words indicate the whole by way of correspondence and also indicate the part likewise, as the word 'possibility' for example: it indicates specific possibility by way of correspondence, and also its part – that is, general possibility – likewise. In the third, it is the application of the word for the implicant (malzum) to the necessary concomitant (lazim) insofar as it is its concomitant, and this condition is also among the entailments. For some words indicate the implicant by way of correspondence and also indicate its necessary concomitant, as the word 'sun' – it indicates the disc of the sun by way of correspondence, and its necessary concomitant – which is the light falling on stones and trees – likewise. The word in this section is that which indicates by way of correspondence, and it is the reality (haqiqah). For reality and metaphor are descriptions of the word, and it is not described by this or that except by the speaker's intent and will. For if it is used to define what it was originally coined for, it is reality; otherwise, it is metaphor – i.e., it is used correctly. And reality is known as: 'the word used to define what it was coined for,' and it is not known as 'every word such and such,' for that is invalid, and its invalidity is apparent upon reflection. Metaphor is known as: 'the word used to define something other than what it was coined for, due to a relation between them,' as between 'lion' and 'brave man' for example. The word in inclusion (tadammun) therefore is metaphor, and likewise in implication (iltizam). The concomitant (lazim) is mental, not external; for the word 'substance,' for example, is not used to define 'accident' even though accident is a necessary concomitant of it, nor is the word 'accident' used to define 'substance' even though substance is a necessary concomitant of it.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the three types of indication (مطابقة, تضمن, التزام) and clarifies the distinction between literal (حقيقة) and metaphorical (مجاز) usage. The author first asserts that the first type, correspondence, is conventional (وضعية), while the other two are rational (عقلية). He explains that the second (inclusion) cannot exist without the first, but the first can exist without the second, as in the word 'substance' indicating its meaning without necessarily implying inclusion. However, the first cannot exist without the third (implication), and vice versa. Then the author addresses a potential objection: why isn't the division expanded to five forms due to metonymy? He argues that metonymy ultimately falls under correspondence if the literal meaning is intended, so the foundational three remain. He then provides examples: 'long-necked' is used literally for length of neck, even if figuratively for height. Next, he defines the literal use as applying a word to what it was coined for, and metaphorical use as applying to something else due to a relation. He gives examples: 'lion' for a brave man. He classifies تضمن and التزام as metaphorical. Finally, he emphasizes that the necessary concomitant (لازم) is mental, not external, because we do not use 'substance' to define 'accident' even though they are related.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalala): Indication; the relationship between a word and its meaning, here divided into three types: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام. [Note: No significant shift across the tradition.]
مطابقة (mutabaqa): Correspondence; the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was coined, used literally. [Note: Stable term across logic texts.]
تضمن (tadammun): Inclusion; the word indicates a part of its meaning, used metaphorically. [Note: Consistently used as one of the three indications.]
التزام (iltizam): Implication; the word indicates something necessarily entailed by its meaning, used metaphorically. [Note: The mental vs external distinction is emphasized here.]
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal usage; a word used for what it was originally coined for. [Note: Stable; but sometimes contrasted with مجاز in terms of frequency.]
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; a word used for something other than its original coinage due to a relation. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements about metaphor (majaz) is correct?
A) It is the use of a word for its original coinage.
B) It is the use of a word for something other than its original coinage due to a relation.
C) It only applies to inclusion (tadammun), not implication (iltizam).
D) It is a rational indication, not conventional.

Key Takeaway
Tadammun and iltizam are metaphorical because they use the word for something other than its original meaning, though with a relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following usages as haqiqa or majaz according to this passage: (a) 'أنزلنا' (We sent down) for revelation, (b) 'أسد' (lion) for a brave man, (c) 'شمس' (sun) for its light. Explain which type of indication each uses.

The Three Types of Signification: Mutabaqa, Tadammun, and Iltizam
Author defines the three types of signification and their binary/ternary relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) in any Arabic word used in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A single word can indicate its full meaning, a part, or a necessary concomitant — that is the triple nature of signification.

The passage you are about to study introduces the three fundamental types of signification (dalala) that every word carries. Understanding these is essential for interpreting Quranic terms, legal definitions, and theological discourse precisely. The author distinguishes between conformity (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam), showing how they relate to each other in binary and ternary patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate the Arabic terms for the three types of signification: al-mutabaqa, al-tadammun, al-iltizam. Notice how the author names them at the end of the passage.
These terms are foundational for all later discussions of signification.
2
Understand the binary relationships
The passage describes three pairs of shared features. For each pair, note what the two types share and how they differ from the third. This is a structural comparison.
For example, mutabaqa and tadammun share that the word does not indicate something outside the meaning.
3
Apply to an example
Take the word 'insan' (human). Using the definitions, determine what each type signifies: the whole human (mutabaqa), the part 'animal' or 'rational' (tadammun), and a necessary concomitant like 'risibility' (iltizam).
This example illustrates how one word carries multiple significations.
4
Verify the definitions
Confirm that mutabaqa indicates the entire meaning, tadammun indicates a part, and iltizam indicates a necessary concomitant that the mind transfers to.
The author emphasizes that these distinctions are clear to a rational person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثنائية فالأول منها وهو الذي يكون بطريق المطابقة يشارك الثاني في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على ما يكون خارجًا عن مسمى اللفظ لازما كان ذلك الخارج أو غير لازم بينا كان أو غير بين. ويشارك الأول الثالث في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على ما يكون داخلا في المسمى عاما كان ذلك الداخل أو خاصا، قريباً كان أو بعيدًا. وكذلك الثاني يشارك الثالث في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على المسمى أصلًا. وعلى هذا في المناسبات فإنها قد تكون ثلاثية وقد كان اللفظ في كل واحد منها يدل على شيء لا يدل على ذلك الشيء غيره، وقد تكون ثنائية كذلك. والأول منها لا يشارك الثاني في شيء إلا ويباين الثالث في ذلك الشيء، وكذلك في الثاني منها والثالث. وأما المناسبات فقد مر ذكرها من قبل، لما مر ذكر اللازمية والملزومية بينها. وهذه الأمور كلها من جملة ما لا يشتبه على العاقل بعد الاطلاع على حقائق تلك الأقسام. ثم الأول منها يسمى بالمطابقة لانطباق كل فرد من أفراد هذا اللفظ على كل فرد من أفراد هذا المعنى، فلا يمكن أن يتجاوز هذا اللفظ عن المسمى في هذه الدلالة البتة؛ والثاني يسمى بالتضمن؛ لما أن المسمى متضمن للمدلول عليه في هذه الدلالة؛ والثالث بالالتزام لما أن المدلول عليه لازم من لوازم المسمى على وجه ينتقل الذهن من المسمى إليه. فكما أن اللفظ يدل على المسمى على التفسير الذي مرَّ ذكره فكذلك المسمى على ما يكون من اللوازم في قسمي الثاني منها والثالث.
As for the binary [relations], the first of them, which is the one that is by way of conformity, shares with the second in that the word in each of them does not indicate what is outside the meaning of the word, whether that outside is necessary or not necessary, evident or not evident. And the first shares with the third in that the word in each of them does not indicate what is inside the meaning, whether that inside is general or specific, near or far. Likewise, the second shares with the third in that the word in each of them does not indicate the meaning at all. And accordingly, with respect to relations, they may be ternary, and the word in each of them indicates something that nothing else indicates, and they may be binary as well. And the first of them does not share with the second in anything except that it differs from the third in that thing, and likewise for the second and third. As for the relations, their mention has preceded, regarding the mention of necessitation and being necessitated between them. And all these matters are among those that are not ambiguous to a rational person after awareness of the realities of those divisions. Then the first of them is called conformity (mutabaqa) because every individual of this word applies to every individual of this meaning, so it is impossible for this word to go beyond the meaning in this indication at all. The second is called inclusion (tadammun) because the meaning includes that which is indicated in this indication. The third is called implication (iltizam) because that which is indicated is a necessary concomitant of the meaning in such a way that the mind transfers from the meaning to it. Just as the word indicates the meaning according to the interpretation mentioned, so too the meaning [indicates] what is among the concomitants in the second and third divisions.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of signification (dalala) of a word: mutabaqa (conformity), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The author begins by explaining the binary and ternary relationships among these types. He notes that mutabaqa and tadammun share that the word does not indicate something outside the meaning, while mutabaqa and iltizam share that the word does not indicate something inside the meaning. Tadammun and iltizam share that the word does not indicate the meaning itself. Then he names each: mutabaqa indicates the whole meaning, tadammun indicates a part contained in the meaning, and iltizam indicates a necessary concomitant. The author emphasizes that these distinctions are clear to a rational person. For example, the word 'insan' signifies the whole human (mutabaqa), also signifies 'animal' by inclusion (tadammun), and signifies 'rational' by implication (iltizam).

Key Terms

التضمن
الالتزام

Key Takeaway
A word's signification is threefold: the full meaning, its part, and a necessary concomitant — and these are distinct in what they exclude.

Ranking the Three Types of Signification: Mutabaqa, Tadammun, Iltizam
Comparison of strength and conventional vs intellectual signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the three types of signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) by strength and explain the rationale for the hierarchy based on the text.

Core Idea
Mutabaqa is the origin; tadammun is stronger than iltizam, and near parts beat far parts.

In this passage, the author sets out to compare the strength of the three types of signification. He presents a clear hierarchy: the signification of conformity (mutabaqa) is strongest because it is the basis for all others, followed by inclusion (tadammun), and then implication (iltizam). Additionally, he notes that signifying near parts or near concomitants is stronger than signifying remote ones. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for determining which meaning takes precedence when analyzing words in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall: mutabaqa (conformity), tadammun (inclusion), iltizam (implication). Locate them in the Arabic.
2
Extract the comparative statements
Read the first sentences: 'دلالة المطابقة أقوى من دلالة التضمن ... ودلالة التضمن أقوى من دلالة الالتزام'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Mutabaqa is the origin (أصل لما عداها); tadammun is like knowledge of the essence, which is prior to knowledge of concomitants.
4
Apply the near vs far principle
The author says near parts are stronger than far parts, and near concomitants stronger than far concomitants. Why? Because they are more directly connected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل في ترجيح البعض منها على البعض؛ وذلك أن دلالة المطابقة أقوى من دلالة التضمن لما أنها أصل لما عداها، ودلالة التضمن أقوى من دلالة الالتزام لما أن العلم نحو الماهية يجري مجرى العلم بالماهية. وعلى هذا دلالة اللفظ على الأجزاء القريبة أقوى من الدلالة على الأجزاء البعيدة، وعلى اللازم القريب أقوى من الدلالة على اللازم البعيد. وقد يقال فيه دلالة التضمن والالتزام من الدلالات العقلية، والدلالة العقلية أقوى من الدلالة الوضعية، وهي دلالة المطابقة لما أن العقلية لا تكون قابلة للتبدل والتغير. غير أنه في حيز المنع لما أنهما وإن كانتا من الدلالات العقلية فحصولهما يفتقر إلى حصول الوضعية، ولأن الترجيح لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون البعض منها في مقابلة البعض، وعند ذلك تكون الوضعية أقوى من العقلية فيما نحن فيه. ثم الوضعية لا تكون إلا على مرتبة واحدة لما أنها بحسب الوضع فلا يكون وجودها بدونه على خلاف العقلية في دلالتي التضمن والالتزام فإنه يمكن في التضمن أن يدل اللفظ على الجزء وعلى جزء ذلك الجزء، كما في لفظ الإنسان فإنه يدل على الحيوان وعلى جزئه وهو الحساس المتحرك بالإرادة كذلك. وكذلك في الالتزام، فإنه يمكن أن يدل على اللازم وعلى لازم ذلك اللازم أيضًا، كما في لفظ الحيوان فإنه يدل على المتحرك بالقوة وعلى لازمه وهو المتحيز بالقوة كذلك. والانتقال في دلالة التضمن أنها من جملة ما ليس بها مراتب وإلا لكان أول الأجزاء في التأليف آخرها في الدلالة. وحينئذ يلزم تأخير فهم الجزء عن فهم الكل، وذلك على خلاف الأصل فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. وقد كان الذي يدل على المسمى من الألفاظ فإنه لا يدل على كل جزء من أجزائه، بل يدل على كل جزء ينتقل الذهن من المسمى إليه، ودلالة اللفظ على كل جزء من تلك الأجزاء على السواء فلا يتقدم البعض منها على البعض في الدلالة.
And it has been said regarding the preference of some of them over others: that the signification of conformity is stronger than the signification of inclusion because it is the origin for what is other than it, and the signification of inclusion is stronger than the signification of implication because knowledge of the essence is akin to knowledge of the essence. And accordingly, the word's signification of near parts is stronger than its signification of far parts, and of the near concomitant stronger than the far concomitant. And it may be said that the significations of inclusion and implication are intellectual significations, and the intellectual signification is stronger than the conventional signification, which is the signification of conformity, because the intellectual is not subject to change and alteration. However, this is in the realm of denial because, even though they are intellectual significations, their occurrence depends on the occurrence of the conventional signification, and because preference cannot be except that one of them is in contrast to the other, and in that case the conventional is stronger than the intellectual in our subject. Then the conventional is only on one level because it is according to convention, so its existence does not occur without it, unlike the intellectual in the two significations of inclusion and implication, for it is possible in inclusion that the word signifies the part and also the part of that part, as in the word 'man' for it signifies 'animal' and its part, which is the sensing and moving by will, likewise. And similarly in implication, it is possible for it to signify the necessary and also the necessary of that necessary, as in the word 'animal' for it signifies 'moving by potential' and its necessary, which is 'occupying space by potential', likewise. And the transition in the signification of inclusion is that it is among those that have no levels, otherwise the first parts in composition would be last in signification, and then the understanding of the part would be delayed after the understanding of the whole, contrary to the principle, for it is not necessary that it be so. And the word that indicates the meaning does not indicate every part of its parts, but rather indicates every part that the mind transfers from the meaning to it, and the word's signification of each of those parts is equal, so some of them do not take precedence over others in signification.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage critically examines the relative strength of the three types of signification. First, it presents an opinion that mutabaqa is strongest because it is the basis, and tadammun is stronger than iltizam. It also notes that near parts and near concomitants have stronger signification than far ones. Another opinion holds that tadammun and iltizam are intellectual significations, and intellectual is stronger than conventional (mutabaqa) because it is unchanging. The author refutes this: intellectual significations depend on conventional signification for their existence, and in a direct comparison, conventional is stronger. Then he explains that conventional signification is only on one level, while intellectual significations can have multiple layers (e.g., a word can signify a part and a part of that part). He gives examples: 'insan' signifies 'hayawan' (animal) by inclusion, and 'hayawan' signifies 'sensing and moving by will' as a part of its part. Similarly, 'hayawan' signifies 'moving by potential' and by implication 'occupying space by potential'. Finally, he discusses that in tadammun there are no distinct levels of signification because if there were, the part composed first would be signified last, which would delay understanding of the part after the whole, which is not the case. Thus, the word signifies each part equally without one preceding another.

Key Terms
الدلالة العقلية
الدلالة الوضعية
المطابقة
التضمن
الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which type of signification is the strongest?
A) Tadammun
B) Iltizam
C) Mutabaqa
D) Intellectual

Key Takeaway
The strength of signification follows this order: mutabaqa (strongest) > tadammun > iltizam, with near parts and concomitants being stronger than far ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Consider the Arabic word 'kitab' (book). Identify which type of signification gives the meaning 'a collection of written pages' (mutabaqa), 'the words inside' (tadammun), and 'the author's intent' (iltizam). Then rank these three significations from strongest to weakest according to the author's hierarchy.
